CHAPTER 1
INTRODUCTION
1.1   Purpose of Study and Terms of Reference
In 1992 a full-scale EIA report was prepared for the XLD multipurpose dam project, together
with an Executive Summary (Ref. 101, Annex 1-1). This was approved by the Government and by
the World Bank (WB) and served the EIA needs for XLD Loan Agreement No. 1 between the
Government and WB.
Loan Agreement No. 2 is now being processed, and the WB has scheduled its appraisal
mission for XLD/Loan 2 for March 1997.
The purpose of the present report is to update the 1992 EIA report to meet the EIA needs for
XLD/Loan 2, including (i) review of actual XLD project performance in carrying out the EPMs
(Environmental Protection Measures) specified in EIA/92, including environmental monitoring, with
presentation of evidence to show that adequate attention has been given to each EPM/monitoring, and
(ii) presentation on how this performance will be continued and, based on experience gained in the
Loan 1 phase, how this performance will be strengthened for the Loan 2 phase.
The TOR for the present report is included here as Annex 1-2.
The basic XLD project and the affected environmental study area (ESA) are the same as
described in the 1992 EIA, as shown in Figure 1.1-1. This shows the project location, the affected
river waterways, and resettlement areas.
1.2    Ornanization of Present Revort
The present report's organization uses the pattern and format developed by WB over the past
several years for EIA for major water resources projects in China, as follows:
Chapter 1:   Introduction
Chapter 2:    Project Description
Chapter 3:    Environmental Setting
Chapter 4:    Environmental Assessment
Chapter 5:    Environmental Monitoring
Chapter 6:    Environmental Management
Chapter 7:    Summary and Conclusions
For purpose of comparison the Table of Contents for the 1992 EIA Executive Summary is
included here as Annex 1-3.
Each chapter of the report contains its own detailed Table of Contents including (i) Text, (ii)
Figures (and where needed, Maps), (iii) Tables, (iv) Annexes, and (v) when needed, Supplemental
Annexes. The Annexes are included in the chapter, but all of the Supplemental Annexes, because of
their many pages, are presented in a separate report volume (Volume II).
1.3   Study Anproach and Strategy
The approach and strategy used for the present study is, not to repeat the detailed information
given in the 1992 EIA, but to make use of it for updating purposes, including brief re-discussion of
basic background items but with attention focused on the critical issue of evaluating adequacy of
management of EPMs delineated in the 1992 EIA, plus management of any additional EPMs
discovered by the subsequent experience as also needing attention.
The 1992 EIA included delineation of an Environmental Management Plan, for giving
attention to all of the designated EPMs. It required (i) establishment of an Environmental
Management Office (EMO) to be responsible for carrying out the EMP, and (ii) establishment of an
Expert Panel on Environment and Resettlement with responsibility for meeting every six months for
the purpose of reviewing the project's environmental performance on Environment and on
Resettlement, with a subpanel for each, with reports submitted both to the Government and the WB.
Responsibility for proceeding with construction of the project was assigned to the Yellow River
Water and Hydroelectric Development Corporation (YRWHDC), and for the Environmental and
Resettlement operation to YRWHDC's Resettlement Office (RO). The RO includes a suboffice for
Resettlement and a suboffice for the EMO, called the Environmental Resource Division or ERD, as
1- I



shown in Figure 1.3-1. The RO/ERD commenced operations in April 1994 (prior to that the RPDI
team which prepared the 1992 EIA served as the interim EMO).
The Expert Panel was established in 1994 and made its initial visit (about 2 weeks per visit)
in July 1994. Figure 1.3-2 shows the Panel members and organization. The functioning of the EMO
and Environmental subpanel are dicussed in Chapter 4.
An important aspect of the environmental assessment work done by ERD is to recognize that
this assignment given to the ERD represents a very formidable job, actually pioneering in China for
major water resource development projects, that ERD's staff and ERD's contract consultants helping
on this work have limited previous experience in this type of assignment (hence have been "learners"
to a considerable degree), and that the only Environmental Expert available to assist and train them
has been the Environmental Engineering Member of the Expert Panel. Hence it has not been possible
for the ERD staff/consultants to carry out all the needed work from the outset, rather they have been
developing this expertise as the construction period has proceeded. Moreover, because of the lessons
learned in the 1992-97 period, the ERD staff/consultants are now in a much better position to carry
out the ERD job and to achieve steady improvements over the Loan 2 period.
1.4   EIA Team
The present EIA has been prepared essentially by RDPI, by a team comprising members and
skills as shown in Figure 1.4- 1. In addition considerable assistance was given to the team by several
experts furnished by the WB.
1.5    References Utilized
Annex 1-1 lists the references which were utilized in preparing the present report, including
background references up to 1992, including the 1992 EIA and subsequent pertinent reports and
publications. There include all of the reports made over the past two years by the EMO staff and its
contractors and by the Expert Environmental Subpanel. Also included are some of the reports
prepared recently at Taiyuan on the EIA aspects of the WB's pending major Wanjiazhai Water
Transfer Project, which contain information applicable to the XLD/Loan 2 phase.
1.6    Acknowledgments
While the RPDI team has had basic responsibility for preparing the present EIA, much of the
information utilized was obtained from the cooperation of (i) other components of the overall Yellow
River Conservancy Commission complex, especially the YRWHDC/RO, (ii) the experts furnished by
the World Bank, (iii) the EPBs of Henan and Shanxi provinces, and (iv) the EIA Division of NEPA at
Beijing.

ANNEX 1-2
TOR FOR XLD/EIA11997
(Memo of 17 Jan 97 from D. Gunaratnum to Wang Xianru)
UPDATE OF ENVIRONMENTAL IMPACT ASSESSMENT
The Environmental Impact Assessment must be updated. The updated EIA will require the following:
(a)  Background and Project Description
(b)  Environment Summary of the Reservoir and Affected Areas (Reservoir Area, Downstream
Area, Estuary and Delta Area). Revisions to information presented in original EIA. There
should be a detailed description of the worsening of downstream drying of the Yellow River
towards the delta and most recent flood damage to farm fields and infrastructure within the
dikes due to high sediment flows in 1992-1996
(c)  Environmental Assessment and Protection Measures (summarized) and Changes in Present
Assessment.
(i)  Dam stability against earthquakes (stability analysis should be updated based on the
information collected from the seismometers installed on site)

CHAPTER 2
PROJECT DESCRIPTION
2.1    Proiect Concept and Components
The XLD Project's overall plan and its components remain essentially the same as described
in the 1992 EIA (Ref. 101). The overall project includes (i) the dam structures per se, including
power generation facilities, for which construction is underway, (ii) the resettlement component,
which is proceeding to match dam construction progress, (iii) the special environmental protection
components, such as relics protection program, disease control program, etc., and (iv) other project
engineering components such as facilities for community/industrial water supply and irrigation, for
which construction has not yet started.
Section 2.3 gives a brief description of the project's major components, which are essentially
the same as in the 1992 EIA.
2.2    Critical Need for Proiect and Proiect Alternatives
2.2.1   Need for Proiect
While the XP is multipurpose and thus will furnish many urgently needed benefits, its major
urgency is its provisions for flood control to protect some 103 million downstream population and
the associated settlements, industry, and agriculture. This problem is especially serious because of
the very heavy silt load of the river resulting in continuing downstream sedimentation so that the
downstream diking system offers protection only against I in 60 year floods and even this system is
in precarious condition. Thus the XLD Project is designed to protect downstream population
settlements, farms, and infrastructure from the hazards of disastrous floods which have plagued this
region for centuries. In addition it will achieve a major expansion in downstream irrigation, will
produce sufficient electric power and water supply for supporting urbanization/industrialization and
for enhancing estuarine ecology, will contribute significantly to expanding downstream aquaculture,
and will furnish additional selected environmental enhancement measures. In spite of the problem of
resettling approximately 181,050 people, the XLD Project will not result in any significant adverse
environmental effects provided the Environmental Management Plan (EMP) specified in the 1992
EIA and again in the present EIA is implemented as delineated in Chapter 6. The primary
beneficiaries of these improvements will be all of the people in the lower basin and particularly the
predominately rural population.
The Yellow River is the muddiest major river in the world because of the high and
continuing massive erosion of the Loess Plateau upstream of the Project area. While the Yellow
River has only 1/20 of the flow volume of the Yangtze River, it carries about three times as much silt
on an annual basis, about 37 kg/m3. In order to protect the flood plain areas in the lower river reaches
from disastrous floods, the existing dikes and training works along both banks must be maintained
and raised on a regular basis. The dike system is very precarious because the river bed is raised or
"suspended" from 3 to as much as 10 meters above the surrounding urban and agricultural areas. It is
increasingly difficult, dangerous and costly to continue raising the dikes because of foundation and
structural safety problems.
The Xiaolangdi Project is critically needed because, as noted above, the present dyke system
protects the flood plain only against floods of a 60 year magnitude. The XLD Project will offer
virtually complete protection in the downstream reaches against the I in 1,000 year flood,
representing enormous flood control benefits.
2.2.2 Proiect Alternatives
The question of project alternatives is discussed in detail in the 1992 EIA. All project
alternatives, described in Figure 2.2-1, were evaluated in detail and the project as formulated in the
1992 Feasibility Study/EIA was selected as the most feasible. Experience since 1992 confirms this
conclusion.
2.3    Summary Proiect Description
2.3.1  Overview
2 - I



The proposed site of the XLD dam is situated at the outlet of the last valley in the middle
Yellow River, 41 km north of the city of Luoyang, Henan Province. The site is 130 km downstream
from the Sanmenxia Dam, and upstream of the Huang-Huai-Hai Plain, with the south bank governed
by Mengjin County and the north bank by Jiyuan City. The dam location is ideal to control silt and
water flow from the main portion of the drainage area, and the dam site is the only one possible
downstream of Sanmenxia to offer sufficient reservoir and flood control capacity for meeting the
present needs. Future planning for additional dams on the Yellow River main stem are discussed in
Section 4.2.8.3.
The dam catchment area is 694,155 km2 , or 92.3% of the Yellow River basin. With the
normal pool level set at El 275 m, the reservoir will provide a total storage capacity of 12.65 billion
m3, covering a surface area of 272 km2, and involving 8 counties/cities including Mengjin, Jiyuan,
Xinan, Mianchi, and Shanxian in Henan Province, and Yuanqu, Pinglu, and Xiaxian in Shanxi
Province.
2.3.2  Functions
The XP has a number of objectives, of which the most important is flood control including
ice run prevention and reducing downstream siltation. Important secondary benefits include
improved water supply, irrigation, and power generation. The overall project is an important element
in the overall plan for achieving optimal economic-cum-environmental harnessing and management
of the water resources of the Yellow River.
2.3.3  Proiect Layout
The XP consists of the dam, flood release and silt discharge structures, and water diversion
and electricity generating structures. The dam is a rockfilled structure with inner blanket and inclined
clay core. The maximum dam height is 154 m, with a crest width of 15 m and a crest length of 1,317
m. The dam includes 3 orifice tunnels for energy dissipation, 3 sediment tunnels, and 6 power
tunnels. The hydroelectric plant is underground with an installed capacity of 1,800 MW (6 x 300
MW). A saddle dam with a clay core is placed on the north bank, and an emergency spillway is
located north of this saddle dam.
The XP requires large quantities of materials. A total of 33.49 million m3 earth and rock
excavation, and 42.84 million m3 embankment will be completed during the construction period. The
works to be completed also include 16 tunnels of various types and sizes. The overall construction
stage will last 8 years (1994 - 2001). The annual average number of construction workers will be
approximately 5,480, with a maximum of 8,540. Construction activities will be mainly completed
with mechanical equipment with the help of manual efforts. A total of 2,724 various machines will be
engaged, requiring a total of 311,830 t fuel and 31,200 t explosives. The peak work intensity will take
place in the 4th year of the construction period.
Figure 2.3-1 and 2.3-2 illustrate the project layout and key features.
2.3.4  Access to Site
The dam site enjoys easy access, with a highway on the south bank accessible to Luoyang
and the Long-Hai Railway, and a 13 km road on the north accessible to Liuzhuang Railway Station
on the Jiao-Zhi Railway. The site is relatively close to such industrial centers as Luoyang, Jiaozuo,
and Xinxiang.
2.3.5  Engineering Design Parameters
The main indices and features of XP are presented in Tables 2.3-1 and 2.3-2.
2.3.6  Proiect Economics
Table 2.3-3 summarizes the pertinent data.
2.4   Proiect Progress
Construction of the XP was approved by the 4th session of the 7th National People's
Representative Congress on April 9, 1991. Commencement of the main works was announced by
Premier Li Peng on September 24, 1994, and progress has proceeded on or ahead of schedule. By the
end of 1996, a total of 22.8 million earth and rock excavation works have been completed,
accounting for 90% of the target to be met prior to the River Diversion in October 1997. The focus of
construction has now changed from earth and rock excavation to concrete placement.
2 -2



The Yellow River Contractor, which is responsible for Lot 1 - Main Dam, has successfully
completed the right foundation excavation and treatment, and has started the dam embankment ahead
of schedule. The completed dam embankment totals 7.19 million m3, constituting 60% of the target
for River Diversion. The responsibility for Lot II is with CGIC Joint Venture, which works jointly
with Ist, 3rd, 4th and 14th Construction Bureaus under MWR, which has overcome various
geological, technical, and managerial difficulties, and has completed the excavation and high-slope
rock mass consolidation works involved in the Diversion Tunnels, Intakes, Plunge Pool, and
Powerhouse Tailrace Channel. The contract for Lot III (Power Facilities) has been awarded to the
Xiaolangdi Joint Venture, which has applied a "cross excavation" method to the Powerhouse
construction, thus effectively handling problems of excavation and rock support activities. As a
result, the Powerhouse Crown excavation and rock cable installation have been completed as
scheduled. The upper portions of all the 6 Power Tunnels and the arch of the Main Transformer
Chamber have also been completed. In addition, excavation has been finished for the LCB No. 2, 3,
and 4 Grout Tunnels.
It is now planned to complete River Diversion on October 31, 1997, and the overall project in
2001. Figure 2.4-1 shows the overall construction schedule from 1994 to 2001, including the dam
project per se and the resettlement program.
2.5    Resettlement Component
2.5.1  General Description
At the design normal pool level of 275 m, the reservoir will inundate 8 counties/cities
respectively distributed in Henan (namely Mengjin, Jiyuan, Xinan, Mianchi and Shanxian) and in
Shanxi (including Yuanqu, Pinglu and Xiaxian). The 1994 population census indicates that the total
population to be affected at that time to be 175,591. The reservoir inundation indices also include
7.4317 million m2 housing, 416,800 mu land including 200,700 mu farmland (48.2%) and 32,900
mu forestland (7.9%), 10,224 km roads, 917.3 km high-voltage power lines, 777.6 km
telecommunications lines, and 893.4 km public address lines.
Figures 2.5-1 illustrates the overall resettlement program. This shows that the resettlement
areas have been and will be available in time to match the needs for the various resettlement phases.
2.5.2 Progress of Resettlement Component
2.5.2.1 Phase I (Construction Area Resettlement)
The construction area resettlement, with a total population of 9,730, who originally lived in
Jiyuan, Mengjin, and the suburb and Jili District of Luoyang, was completed at the end of 1994. As
discussed in Chapter 4 and based on the investigation and evaluation carried out by NCWCHPI and
the Rural Investigation Teams of affected counties, both residential and living conditions at the new
sites are significantly improved, with water/power supply, clinic, school, road, and other public
facilities considerably improved as compared with the resettlers' previous situations.
2.5.2.2 Phase II
Phase II resettlement from the reservoir area involves 27 administrative villages governed by
7 xiangs (townships) in Mengjin, Jiyuan, and Xinan. The total affected population is 46,000, with a
total of 1.6170 million m2 housing and 119,000 mu land inundated. The resettlement planning was
completed in October 1994 and approved by the central government at the end of 1995. Up to the end
of 1996, the 16,580 resettlers form 4,478 families have all been moved to agricultural areas where the
new sites have easy access, fertile land, and abundant water resources, with more than 0.8 mu
irrigated land available for each of the resettlers. Both projection and livelihood are significantly
improved. All components of Phase II resettlement are scheduled to be completed by the end of June
1997.
2.5.2.3 Phase III
Phase III will involve a total population of 118,985, with 83,066 resettlers from Henan
Province and 6,682 resettlers from Shanxi Province. From the end of 1996, the resettlement had been
started. The detailed implementation plan for this phase is to be completed by the end of June 1997
and the implementation for Phase III is scheduled to be completed by the end of 1999.
2.5.2.4 Phase IV
2 - 3



In accordance with the requirement of State Planning Commission, the detailed
implementation plan for this phase should be completed by the end of June 1997 and the resettlement
from the reservoir should be completed by the end of 2003.
2.6    Environmental Protection Components
The overall XP includes, in addition to the project's engineering components and
resettlement component, a number of environmental protection components, including the
archeological relics protection program, communicable disease control program, and others.
2.6.1  Relics Protection Program
The middle reaches area of the Yellow River basin is known as the "cradle" of the Chinese
nation. There are many cultural/historical relics scattered over the region. In accordance with the
results from the investigations carried out by Henan/Shanxi Provincial Archeological Institutes
(HSAls), there are altogether 109 sites, 29 above the ground and the rest underground, below El. 275
in the area around the damsite and reservoir area.
For the purpose of cultural relics protection and salvation, a detailed drilling, evaluation, and
excavation plan has been developed and included in the overall plan of XP. The plan is delineated in
Section 4.2.4. By the end of December 1996, excavations and salvation had been carried out in a total
surface area of 7,300 m2, including 160 ancient tombs, I jar coffin, and 40 ash pits. Archeological
works completed in the reservoir area, as described in Section 4.2.4, include field surveys of ancient
roads and canals built during the period from Han to Tang Dynasties, investigation of ancient
structures in 4 villages, preparation of 156 drawings, and 500 pictures.
2.6.2  Disease Control and Other Protection Measures
2.6.2.1 Communicable Disease Control
Communicable diseases in the reservoir area and the lower reaches of the river include
malaria, encephalitis, hemorrhagic fever, enteric diseases, and others as discussed in Chapter 3. As
the building of the dam reservoir will change the hydrological condition in these regions, EIA/1992
included detailed assessment and proposed disease control measures. The magnitude and other
relevant parameters of such impacts are under continuing monitoring so that necessary corrective
measures can be taken to avoid any increase of incidence of these diseases because of the project.
Information on the pertinent diseases is given in Chapters 3 and 4.
2.6.2.2 Earthauake Hazards
Considering the seriousness of dam failure subsequent to seismic event, provision for dam
stability against earthquakes has been a major aspect of the project design. The proposed dam will be
able to withstand the expected maximum earthquake in the dam site area, For facilitating seismicity
prediction, a seismic network has been established, comprising of seismoscopic stations, I repeater
center, and I data processing center located in Mengjin, Xinan, Jiyuan, and Luoyang. The location of
the seismoscopic stations is shown on Figure 2.6-1. The service area of the network covers some
1,400 km2, ranging 40 km upstream to 8 km downstream of the dam site. Within this area, any
significant earthquake can be reported and the epicenter positioning accuracy can be controlled at 0.5
km. The network had been completed and put into operation from November 1, 1995. The
performance of the network over the past years shows that the design is rational, and that operation of
the network is reliable and is meeting the requirements for providing seismic protection and warning
for XP.
2.6.3  Environmental Management Plan and Office
An environmental management and implementation plan is the basis for the success of
environmental protection. The XP environmental management plan, carried out by EMO, includes
provisions for carrying out all EPMs specified in the EIA, including environmental monitoring and
reporting, and provisions for training of staff engaged in EMP operation. To ensure good
performance by the EMO, the plan includes institutional and administrative arrangements needed for
facilitating EMO performance.
2.7    Other Proiect Engineering Components
Besides the main purpose of XLD project, namely flood control, the project is also aimed to
benefit the surrounding region and the lower reaches in the respects described below.
2-4



2.7.1  Irrigation
The XLD project is planned to support the expansion of the irrigation area by 350,000 mu, in
the region surrounding the reservoir, and 30 million mu in the lower reaches.
2.7.2  Water Supply
Industrial and municipal water supply in the lower reaches will be improved year by year
when the project is put into operation. This will be possible because XP will permit much more
effective control of the river flow.
2.7.3  Power Production and Distribution
The project includes a power station, with total installed capacity of 1,800 MW, which will
supply annually 5.4 GWiH of hydropower to the Central China Power Grid. Such power generation
will greatly promote economic and local socio-economic development.
2-5

CHAPTER 3
ENVIRONMENTAL SETTING IN ENVIRONMENTAL STUDY AREA
3.1    Overall Environmental Study Area
The overall Environmental Study Area (ESA) which is subject to significant impacts from the
Xiaolangdi Project extends for approximately 1,000 km along the Yellow River, from the Sanmenxia
Darn to the estuary and delta at Bohai Bay. The ESA is described in detail in the ESA/1992 report, and
this information is briefly summarized here. The overall ESA is geographically divided into four areas
for EIA study purposes: (i) the reservoir area and surrounding region above the Xiaolangdi Dam up to
Sanmenxia Dam, (ii) the dam vicinity, (iii) the lower river reaches, and (iv) the estuary, delta, and
nearshore marine regions. The overall ESA is shown in Figure 1.1-1 of Chapter 1 and in Figure 3.1-1.
Subareas are shown in Figures 3.1-2, 3, and 4.
3.2    Reservoir and Vicinity (Figure 3.1-2)
3.2.1   Geoaraohv and Land Use
The reservoir and surrounding area cover approximately 130 km of the river valley and adjacent
hills and mountains, from the Sarumenxia Dam to the Xiaolangdi dam site (Figure 3.1-2). The main
stream of the river through this stretch follows a narrow, deeply incised valley, with little flood plain
development. Some flood plains are located at the mouths of several of the 11 tributaries that enter this
section of the river.
Land uses in the ESA are shown in Figure 3.2.1 - 1.
3.2.2  Climate
The climate is that of a warm temperate zone and continental location, with cold and dry
winters and warm and wet summers. Average annual temperature is 10 - 15C, depending on the local
geography. More than half of the 600 mm annual precipitation falls in mid and late summer, and severe
storms are common. The highest recorded rainfall was 600 mm on July 16, 1958. Flooding from runoff
in the rainy season is a major concern. The measured annual evaporation rate is twice the average
annual rainfall, so that the area is also very susceptible to droughts. In one reservoir-area, Jiyuan City,
there have been droughts in 21 years over a recent 22 year period.
3.2.3   Air Ouality
Air quality in the reservoir and surrounding area is generally within the applicable Chinese
standards. Suspended particles can be somewhat high due largely to dust blown in from the Loess
Plateau and to dust generated locally from agricultural operations. This is particularly evident in the dry
season.
3.2.4   Hydrolojz
Given the importance of flow dynamics in the Yellow River to the economy and hulmlaln
security in the lower Yellow River basin, extensive monitoring and analysis of surface water hydrology
in the main strearn and tributaries has been performed, resulting in observed and synthetic data covering
a complete time series for over 70 years.
With a total catchment of over 750,000 km2, the Yellow River discharge is approximately 43.2
billion m3, with 1.6 billion tons of silt in an average year (1919 - 1960 series). Over 60% of runoff and
80% of sediment occur in the rainy season. The runoff entering into the lower reaches is 45.59 billion
m3/year and silt is 1.5 billion t/year. The runoff and silt at Lijin hydrology station are 38.78 billion m3
and 0.987 billion t/year, respectively. The overall situation on surface water hydrology is discussed in
Chapter 4 Section 4.2.8.1. Information on low and zero flows in the lower river reaches is river here in
Table 3.2.4- 1.
The catchment of the river above the upstream end of the proposed reservoir is approximately
688,000 km2, or 91.5% of the total Yellow River watershed. At this point, the river still has over 1,000
km to travel to the sea. The river and tributaries in the reservoir area contribute some 5,800 m3 of
catchment with a mean annual runoff of 0.56 billion m3. The ratio of peak to minimum river flow over
the year is approximately 3.2. Extended periods have occurred when the annual river flow has been
approximately 20 - 25% higher or lower than the norrn.
3 - I



Flooding is related primarily to rainstorms. Sediment load is highest in flood season and has
been increasing with average height of 45 kg/m3. Its long-term average is 45 kg/m3. The maximum
sediment content recorded was 941 kg/m3 (flood peak amount 9,870 m3/s, Xiaolangdi hydrology
station, August 7, 1977). The dynamics of erosion and sedimentation in the entire Yellow River
watershed have been studied carefully, and are detailed in the EIAJ1992 report.
3.2.5   Geologv. Topographv. and Earthquake Hazard
3.2.5.1 General Geologv and To2ograohv
The reservoir and its surrounding area are bounded by mountainous regions to the north, west
and south, which change gradually to hills to the broad flood plain of the lower Yellow River to the east.
The east-west axis of the Kuangkou anticline passes through the damsite. The northern dam abutrnent
and hydraulic structures will be located on the northem flank of the anticline, while the southern
abutment will be on the anticline's south flank. Less than 5% of the reservoir and surrounding area is
relatively flat, and only 14% has a gradient between 3 - 15. Almost half of the area has a gradient over
250, as shown in Table 3.2.5-1. Figure 3.2.6-1 shows geological features in the reservoir region.
3.2.5.2 Earthauake Hazard
In the Cenozoic era a number of major and minor faults occurred in the project area. Seismic
zones have been identified for the area and earthquakes of magnitude 5 have occurred in the reservoir
area. The damsite area has a seismic intensity level of class 7. The location of earthquake observation in
the reservoir region is shown Figure 2.6-1 of Chapter 2.
3.2.6  Soils and Soil Stability
Loess with a relatively high clay content makes up the majority of the soils found below 1,000
m elevation. These very important agricultural soils are commonly slightly alkaline with only 0.5 - 1%
organic matter. Study of surface geology has enabled potential bank collapse and landslide areas to be
identified, as shown in Figure 3.2.6-1. The most serious of these are located between Hedi and
Lingshang villages near the Banjian river.
3.2.7   Water Ouality
3.2.7.1 Groundwater
The shallow and artesian groundwater in the reservoir area and surrounding area is recharged
by rainfall, and is generally suitable for drinking. The groundwater resources are illustrated in the
hydrogeology date shown in Figure 3.2.7-1.
3.2.7.2 Surface Water
Surface mineral water quality of the Yellow River and its tributaries is generally very good,
even though it has an exceptionally high silt load, and it generally meets the first grade of China's water
use standard at most locations in the ESA. Specifics are given in Section 4.2.7.3 of Chapter 4. Local
pollution is mainly from mining enterprises, with minor contributions from agricultural runoff and
domestic sewage. These result in some localized areas of high BOD, COD, phenols and zinc, mostly
near the tributaries.
3.2.8   Vegetation and Asriculture
The Xiaolangdi area is near the southern edge of the warm temperate zone. This, in
combination with the area's variable geography, results in terrestrial plant communities which can be
relatively complex and diverse. In the reservoir area itself (Figure 3.2.8-1), however, there has been a
long history of settlement and cultivation, and the natural vegetation and diversity have been greatly
diminished.
Crops in the ESA are predominantly wheat, corn, cotton, and oil crops.
3.2.9  Natural Ecological Resources
3.2.9.1 Terrestrial Forests and Wildlife
The situation on terrestrial forests and wildlife is shown in Figure 3.2-5, including location of
protected habitats, and of wild animals, represented in the reservoir area by mostly northern species, are
very low throughout the region (Figure 3.2.9-1). Waterfowl are diverse, but populations are low.
Vegetation and fauna have been studied for the EIA with particular attention to rare and commercial
species. This information is summarized in Table 3.2.9-1.
3.2.9.2 Aquatic Wildlife
3 -2



Due to the exceptional silt content, aquatic communities are marginal in the Yellow River main
stem, hence there are no significant fisheries.
Significant fishery habitat is predominantly located in the tributaries, with some corresponding
contamination of fish from the industrial pollution sources located there. The information on natural
fisheries is summarized in Figure 3.2.9-2 and Table 3.2.9-2.
3.2.10 Mineral Resources
The mineral resources of the region are significant and include valuable reserves of coal,
bauxite, copper, iron and building materials. The reserves most affected by the reservoir will be coal,
bauxite and sulfur-iron. It may be possible to recover the coal, since it is covered by a thick
impermeable layer of shale, and could be mined beneath the reservoir. This information is summarized
in Figure 3.2.10-1.
3.2.11 Demoaraphv and Socio-Economies
The population of the reservoir and surrounding area is about 2.4 million people, approximately
181,050 of which live in the area to be flooded. Agricultural families comprise 90% of the population of
the study area, and over 92% in the area to be flooded. The flat valley lands and hills up to 200 m
elevation are almost entirely devoted to agriculture, the main agricultural types being cereal crops and
cotton. Despite the importance of agricultural enterprises to the regional economy, overall productivity
is not high, and annual per capita agricultural income in the region is 346 yuan.
The total area to be submerged is approximately 272 km2, of which 44% is cultivated. This
represents just over 4% of the cultivated land of the overall study area, but includes the most fertile and
productive areas. With the proposed maximum water level at 275 m elevation, the Xiaolangdi Reservoir
will flood 179 villages, 11 government towns, 93 industrial and mining enterprises, 250 commercial
enterprises and other institutions, 12 power stations, 267 irrigation pumps, 658 km of irrigation canals,
688 km of roads, and 548 km of communication lines.
3.2.12 Public Health
Because of the need to relocate a significant number of people affected by inundation, a careful
study was done of the public health dynamics within the reservoir and study areas. The main public
health problems identified include malaria, diseases related to agricultural practices (such as
Brucellosis), encephalitis B, diseases carried by rats, waterborne diseases, and endemic fluorosis caused
by cooking with the high-fluoride local coal. This picture is summarized in Figure 3.2.12- 1.
3.2.13 Archeological Relics
In view of the importance of the Yellow River basin in the development of Chinese civilization,
a detailed archaeological survey has been completed as described in Section 4.2.4. While none of these
are classified as protected by either the State or the Provinces, many of them are regarded as precious
resources for research on architectural and art history. There are also many important cultural resources
in the area surrounding the reservoir. The picture is summarized in Figure 3.2.13- 1.
3.3    Dam Vicinity
3.3.1  Construction Zone
The construction zone (see Figure 2.3-2 of Chapter 2) covers a surface area approximately 23.7
km2, 18 km in length, extending from Chihetan in the west and reaching Liuzhuang Railway Transfer
Platform, and 8 km in width starting from Dongguanzhuang in the south and ending at the foot of
Wangling mountain in the north. It involves 29 villages distributed in 7 townships governed by Mengjin
County, Luoyang outskirts, Jili District and Jiyuan.
3.3.2   Construction Zone Uses by Topography
In order to match construction according to the project's major features. The main works are
divided into 3 lots: Lot I--Main Dam; Lot II--Intakes, Tunnels and Spillway; Lot III--Power Facilities,
with a separate international contractor responsible for each lot.
In view of the local topography, the construction area is subdivided into the following areas:
(a) Liaowu Area: This area includes floodland and mountain regions. The 35 km2 floodlands
are used as work areas for Lot II and Lot III. Lot II is located in the west portion, covering
about 100,000 m2, and Lot III is arranged in the east portion, covering approximately 160,000
3 -3



m2. The mountain region is occupied by Lot H and Lot III local labor camps which are
separated by a road.
(b) Qiaogou Floodland Area: This part of floodland is divided into left and right regions, with
the right region further subdivided into upper, middle and lower parts. The upper part is used
for expatriate camps and office areas of Lots I, II, and III, while and middle and lower parts are
respectively occupied by YRWHDC Hospital and Lot III spoil areas. In the left region, the
upper part is covered by YRWHDC living quarters and office facilities. The lower part is used
for YRWHDC's parking area and multipurpose service building.
(c) Xiaondnzhuang-Tongshuling Area: This area is used for Lot II local labor camp.
(d) Xiaolangdi Area: Covering a total land area of 230,000 m2, this area includes a concrete
batching plant, a complex processing plant, and some associated facilities.
(e) Siyuanpo Area: This area includes mainly borrow areas and quarries.
(f) Other Areas: These include Huaishuzhuang floodland spoil area, Liandi aggregate
processing plant, and Liuzhuang Railway Transfer Platform.
3.3.3  Transportation
The construction area has convenient transportation conditions. On the south bank are 1#
Access Road, and 2#-5# Access Roads for use of Lot I Contractor. On the north bank are 6#-9# Roads
on Site mainly for use of Lot II and Lot III Contractors. All the Roads on Site have macadam pavement.
These roads are regularly watered to avoid fugitive dust. Also, macadam is spread on a regular basis for
the purpose of road maintenance.
3.3.4  Other Conditions
The meteorology, hydrology, flora, fauna and other physical and social environmental
components are the same as those in the reservoir area and surrounding region.
3.4    Downstream Riverine Area (Figure 3.1-3)
3.4.1  Generl Situation
The area downstream of Xiaolangdi (Figure 3.1-3) includes irrigated areas within and adjacent
to the Yellow River stretching approximately 870 km from the proposed darn to the estuary. Between
Xiaolangdi and the start of the dikes, over some 70 km, the river is broad and flows past Mangshen
mountain to the south. At this point, there are approximately 330 km2 of flood plain to the north. For the
remaining 800 km to the estuary, the river is "suspended" in a raised bed about 3 meters above ground
level, with dikes on both sides except for about 100 km at and downstream of the mouth of the tributary
Dawen River, where there is no dike on the southern bank (roughly 390-290 km from the estuary).
In the reach 128 km from Xiaolangdi downsnteam to the Huayuankou monitoring station, the
Yellow River receives two major tributary systems, the Yiluo and the Qinhe, which together contribute
approximately 36,000 km2 of catchment The quality of the water in these systems is relatively polluted
as indicated by the presence of significant levels of COD and phenols. Water quality in the main river is
thus degraded to the second grade of the Chinese standard, until natural purification restores a first
grade quality at Gaocun station, 579 km from the estuary. Water quality remains good for the rest of the
main stream. Downstream of Huayuankou there is relatively little further addition of catchmnent area
until the final 20,000 km2 is contributed by the Dawen River and the Beijindi detention basin, about 395
km from the estuary. Outside the catchment areas related to tributaries, there is very little runoff, and
essentially no urban waste discharges enter the Yellow River downstream of Zhengzhou due to the
dikes.
The downstream area has a similar general climate to that of the reservoir area, except that it
becomes both warmer (average temperature ranging from 120 - 29C) and wetter (precipitation up to
700 mm/yr from west to east). Precipitation is seasonal, and hence there is the same seasonallity in flow
pattems in the downstream tributaries.
Most of the downstream riverine area falls within the arid and semi-arid zone. The long-term
annual precipitation is 450 mm, only 44% of that in the Yangtze River basin. Moreover, the
precipitation here is unevenly distributed in time and space, which results in the innate water
inadequacy of the Yellow River. On the other hand, more and more water is consumed by the people
living on both banks as a result of social progress, thus significantly reducing the downstream runoff.
3 -4



Natural hazards plus human activities have resulted in frequent zero flow events in the lower river
reaches since 1972, and more importantly the frequency, duration and length of zero flow events have
been continuously increased. At Lijin Hydrometric Station in Shandong Province, for instance, such
events occurred in 18 of the 24-year period starting 1972 and ending 1995, i.e. 3 in each 4 years. In this
period, a total of 49 zero flow events occurred with a total duration of 546 days. Details are described in
Table 3.4.1-1.
Groundwater throughout the downstream area is generally abundant and of good quality.
Figure 3.4.1-1 shows main infrastructure and mineral resources in the downstream region.
The situation on public health/diseases in the downstream area is shown in Figure 3.2.12- 1.
3.4.2  Siltation
Over millennia, the huge quantities of sediment carried and deposited by the Yellow River have
created the Great Northern China Plains, also known as the Hung-Huai-Hai Plains. The course of the
river has changed some 26 times in recorded history (2,500 yrs), sometimes quite drastically. The
present course downstream of Jiahetan was established in 1855, following a major breaching of a dike.
The valley area is broad and relatively flat, though it is constrained to the south in the area of the Tai
Mountains, the source of the Dawen River. The high silt-laden water of the Yellow River has
continuously raised the downstream river bed, thus obstacling flood discharge.
Sedimentation and scouring in the Yellow River have recently undergone some major changes.
Prior to 1950, breaches in the dikes were common resulting in frequent sedimentation outside the dikes.
Since 1950, such events have become less common, and the silt load is now maintained between the
dikes. The Sanmenxia Dam, which began to fill in 1960, has altered sedimentation dynamics
considerably; it has enabled some control to be exerted over deposition and scouring both in the
reservoir and in the downstream reaches. The effects of different operating regimes of the Sanmenxia
Dam have been studied, its basic operating principles have been evaluated, and several successful
alterations made to the damn enable better sediment control.
3.4.3   Natural Ecologv
3.4.3.1 General
The overall flood protection region downstream of Xiaolangdi supports approximately 70
million people. Agriculture is intensive, and the area is the main cotton and wheat production region in
China. Gross agricultural output is valued at 29 billion yuan annually. Industry has been developing
rapidly in the area. Whereas agriculture formerly represented 70% of the region's contribution to the
national economy, industry now contributes 60%. The abundant coal, oil and natural gas reserves in this
region are significant for economic development and urban growth.
3.4.3.2 Wetlands and Forests (Figure 3.2.9-1)
Many wetlands exist in the flood areas between the dikes and are important for aquatic animals
and waterfowl. The wetland in the old course of the river 100 km to the north in Henan Province has
been made into a nature preserve. Such areas support large and diverse bird populations and are
important for migration.
The downstream area has been heavily populated and cultivated for many centuries, and the
natural environment has therefore been replaced by intensive agriculture throughout. There are a few
remaining natural forested areas, especially in the hilly and mountainous Dawen and Taishan areas to
the south. Wildlife animal communities are somewhat more complex than in the Xiaolangdi area,
though northern species still dominate. In the cultivated areas, agricultural pest species such as mice,
rats, and pigeons are prolific.
3.4.3.3 Taishan Mountain
Taishan Mountain in Shandong Province, has been designated a world heritage site by
UNESCO.
3.4.3.4 Fisheries
Because of the river's high silt content there are no significant fisheries in the downstream river
reaches.
3.4.3.5 Rare and Endangered Species
3-5



In the natural reserve, announced by the government in 1995, in the downstreamn areas, there
live such protective animals as waterfowl, Grus japonensis , gray crane, white stork, giant swan, golden
eagle, giant bustard, Accipitridae, tattle, wild goose, gull, and mandarin duck. The protective plants
include wild bean, natural Robinia pseudoacacia forests, natural willow forests, natural reed marshes,
and seepweed. The distribution of some rare and endangered species is shown on Figure 3.2.9-1.
3.5    Estuary, Delta, and Nearshore Marine Areas (Figure 3.1-4)
3.5.1   General Conditions
The estuary and delta comprise approximately 6,000 km2 of flat, lowlying land (Figure 3.1-4).
They have increased by some 2,200 km2 since the Yellow River began discharging here in 1855. Over
700 km2 were added between 1954 and 1984; and over the last 40 years, some 25 - 30 km2/yr have
been added.
Average annual outflow at Lijin hydrology station is approximately 38.78 billion m31yr and
average discharge of silt is 987 million tons/yr, representing 87% and 75.4% of the inflow, respectively.
Siltation of the river channel in the lower reaches is govemed mainly by upstream runoff and sediment
load. Influence on siltation in the river exerted by the extension of the delta has not been significant in
the last 40 years. Water level for equivalent runoff (3,000 m3/s) at the Lijin station (at the head of the
estuary) has risen 1.55 m between 1950 and 1989, which is less than farther upstream.
The climate of the delta is partly moderated by the sea with a 12C average temperature. Winds
are high and storms serious. The area experiences the same cyclic precipitation and runoff patterns as
upstream areas. The low flow period is more frequently exacerbated by increasing water use, with
periods of zero flow being recorded. Such periods adversely affect the estuarine fishery and water use in
the delta, for example for domestic consumption, aquaculture, irrigation, and industrial uses such as
power plants and oil fields in the delta.
Except for the silt content, the water quality is fairly good due to the relatively small amounts of
contamination, and the long run of the lower reaches, which promotes effective natural purification.
The major economic activity in the delta is provided by the Victory Oil Field, which is the
second largest in China, and is undergoing continuing development.
3.5.2 Natural Ecologv
3.5.2.1 Vegetation
Vegetation is typical of the deltaic areas with meadows, marsh plants and hydrophytes
predominating, including halophytic salt tolerant species in coastal and low lying locations. There are
some small wooded areas and cultivated lands along the coast. Even with the relatively small tidal range
(I - 2 m), there are extensive salt marshes and intertidal areas.
3.5.2.2 Aquatic Production
The aquatic production at the estuary and in adjacent sea area is relatively high. Both plankton
and benthos are diverse and abundant in these areas. The estuary is an important spawning and nursery
area for both river and oceanic species of fish, crustaceans and shellfish, which are a valuable and
renewable economic resource.
3.5.2.3 Wetlands
Many wetlands are situated in the coastal depressions where there is abundant natural
precipitation and groundwater. They are noted for aquatic migrants and waterfowl, and some have been
made into natural reserves such as the Wuli, Shouguang, and Weifang natural reserves. Such areas are
important for migrating birds, and a number of species including herons, egrets, swans, geese and
ducks, which winter there in large numbers.

CHAPTER 4
ENVIRONMENTAL ASSESSMENT
4.1    Assessment Methodology
4.1.1  Approach and Strategy
The EIA/1991 report delineated the SEIs and prioritized these according to the inforrnation
available at that time. The ERD, established as the EMO suboffice of YRWHDCRO in 1994, has
over the past two years been busy in getting itself established and getting into operation for carrying
out its job of ensuring that the EPMs specified in EIA/1992, including environmental monitoring, are
actually being carried out in the continuing steps of implementation of the project's components.
These steps comprise (i) final design (detailed plans and specifications and contract documents), (ii)
construction, and (iii) O&M following completion of construction. Thus far Steps (i) and (ii) are
applicable to all project components, but Step (iii) applies only to those RS villages which have been
actually occupied and lived in for a sufficient period to check on environmental adequacy of the
homes and community facilities.
The approach used here is illustrated by Figure 4.1.1-1 which lists each of the SEIs identified
in EIA/92 with prioritization, which shows the status of progress in implementing each of these with
identification of which of the Steps (i), (ii), (iii) above have been subject to evaluation since
establishment of EMO, plus a relisting of these issues/priorities as now seen in 1997. Then each of
these SEls is discussed as a separate section of this chapter, to evaluate the adequacy both of the
work of the EMO and of the actual management of the SEI, and to indicate improvements needed in
performance of the EMO and of the other organizations involved to achieve the needed level of
performance for the XLD/Loan 2 period.
4.1.2  Data Utilized in Evaluation
The data utilized for making the evaluations noted above are shown in Annex 1-1,
References. Of primary use are the periodic reports prepared by EMO staff and subcontractors
(Reference 203) and the periodic reports prepared by Environmental Engineer member of the Expert
Panel (H. Ludwig) (Reference 201), plus various related special reports as listed in the "200 Series"
of references. The EMO reports reflect the EMO's accounts on progress in its work of implementing
the Environmental Management Plan (EMP) stipulated in EIA/92 (and included in the XLD/Loan I
Agreement). The reports of the EEMEP contain periodic evaluations of the performance of EMO
staff/subcontractors and of the adequacy of implementative of the EMP.
Included here as Annex 4.1.2-1 is information taken from the latest report of the Panel's
Environmental Engineer of October 1996, including its Table of Contents and its Chapter
I/Introduction and Chapter 6/Summary and Conclusions. These illustrate the scope of activities
discussed above.
4.1.3  Progressive Development of EMO
Figure 4.1.3-1 shows the institutional set up for YRWHDCRO with its two suboffices, one
the EMO (termed the ERD) and the other the RS operations. Figure 4.1.3-2 gives details on ERD and
its staff and subcontractors as of Feb. 1997.
It is important to note here that the RO "started from scratch" in 1994, and over the period
since then has steadily and progressively built up its ERD staffing and operating mechanics including
use of the several subcontractors, beginning with ERD staff with limited experience in how to plan
and carry out EMO operations. One of the salient roles of the Panel's EEMEP has been, not only to
make the evaluations noted above, but to carry out continuing training/technology transfer for the
ETD staff, and details of this work are given in the References 201 reports. In theory the RO/ERD
should of course be carrying out its EMO functions beginning at time of its establishment in 1994,
but because the EMO job is of large magnitude both in scope and complexity, and is pioneering for a
major dam/reservoir project in China, the procedure has been one of learning-how-to-do-on-the-job
with periodic training inputs from the EEMEP and other members of the Expert Environmental
Subpanel during the meetings of about two weeks held every six months. This has been a difficult
process, and if more training expertise had been furnished (as is now being proposed for the EMO to
4 - I



be established for the pending major Wanjiazhai project), the progress of development of ERD would
of course have been expedited. Considering all of the difficulties and limitations involved, the ERD
has been making meaningful progress and, as will be noted in the detailed discussions of SEIs below,
the ERD is learning how to do its job, but of course more and more is to be learned in the years
ahead. The conclusion here is that consideration should be given to provision of some continuing
training expertise as part of the Loan 2 provisions, following the pattern proposed for the pending
Wanjiazhai Project (Reference 303). This would include training in all steps (i) (ii) (iii) noted in
Section 4.1.1 above. A copy of this proposed Wanjiazhai training is included here as Annex 4.1.3-2.
To sum up, much has been expected of the ERD staff and the staff has worked hard to
endeavor to comply with the Panel's Environmental Engineer's recommendations, but because of
their limited experience, the surveillance program exercised by EMO needed for implementing the
ENT has been only partial, but nevertheless meaningful. As indicated in Reference 201.5 on the
Enviromnental Subpanel's visit of October 1996, the ERD is just about now beginning to round into
shape and this can be regarded as a meaningful accomplishment.

essentially no change in SEI delineation/prioritization except for the addition of the issue of Risk

4.2.2  Environmental Mana;erent of Dam Construction
4.2.2.1 Introduction
This is the 'No. I SEr' for XLD for the construction period, to ensure that the activities of
the Construction Contractors (CCs) do include provision of all EPMs specified in EIA/92. Pursuant
to the EIA, the contract documents include EPMs as specified in the EIA as shown in Supplemental
Annex 4.2.2-1. This has been the primary focus of effort by the ERD since its establishment in 1994,
with the actual work carried out by RPDI as subcontractor. This has also been the primary focus of
effort of the EEMEP in the five Panel visits since July 1994. The RPDI work has been carried out at
the CC sites (Lots I, II, III) by an RPDI team headed by Mme. Xie Xinfang.
4.2.2.2 Applicable Parameters and Performance
Based on all of the pertinent reports (References 200 Series) Figure 4.2.2 -1 has been
prepared to summarize the appropriate SEIs for the construction operations with information on
adequacy of attention by the RPDI team and by the CCs. Examination of this information indicates
the following:
(a) The primary problem which has limited performance by the EMO field staff to date has
been the fact that these staff are in process of developing the capabilities needed for their work,
which is technically complicated and institutionally difficult. This limited the work of the staff
including their reporting, and this in turn has limited their ability to keep EMO/HQ duly informed
and to exercise proper control over the CC, that is, monitoring of the CC operations have been only
partial. However, the staff capabilities have been steadily improving with experience and training
being obtained.
(b) Because of (a), and of limited capabilities of EMOIHQ staff (also in process of training),
plus institutional problems in having to function in an organization with primary focus on
resettlement, the EMO/HQ office has been only partially effective in terrns of (b. 1) obtaining a
definitive picture of CC inadequacies and (b.2) follow-up measures for requiring better performance
by CCs.
(c) The CC staffs have or can furnish all the needed technical capabilities to do their job but,
like most humans, they tend not to do as much as they should when surveillance of their
performance and follow-up pressures from EMO/HQ are lacking. For this reason CC performance
has not been generally sufficient (c. 1) in treatment and disposal of sanitary and industrial wastewater,
especially in terms of their own monitoring related to environmental protection from discharge of
4-2



wastewater effluent, (c.2) in dust control, and (c.3) erosion silt runoff and resurfacing of disturbed
areas.
(d) The principal conclusion of the EEMEP is that the CCs should themselves contribute
much more to assisting EMO in achieving better control by requiring the CCs themselves to submit
monthly reports covering all aspects of their environmental performance, and this program was
recommended by the EEMEP in Oct., 96 and initiated partially by the CCs for the month of January
1997 following the EEMEP's detailed guidelines shown in Supplemental Annex 4.2.2-2. Such
monthly reports include detailed attention to all significant parameters (including water supply,
wastewater management, solid waste management, air pollution and dust management, drainage,
toilet and other sanitation facilities in the residential and working areas, mosquito vector control, rat
control, medical examinations, occupational health, emergency management plans, etc.) plus
attention to (i) any environmental problems experienced during the month and how these were
resolved, and (ii) any notices from EMO received during the month and how these were acted upon.
The system recommended above should be implemented in full as part of the Loan 2
Agreement. This is the system commonly utilized in the USA and other ICs for such monitoring. The
project does the bulk of the monitoring and the role of the government environmental regulators is to
"monitor the monitoring".
Supplemental Annexes 4.2.2-7.1, 7.2, and 7.3 are the reports produced by the three lots for
the month of January. This is the beginning effort. Full-scale reports from all three CCs should
commence with the reports for February. Each of the CCs should appoint an Environmental Officer
to have responsibility for the monthly reporting. This should not be expected of the Safety Officer
because the Environmental and Safety work represent two different fields of expertise.
4.2.2.3 Role of EMO versus XECC Safety Department
Major improvement is needed in how EMO instructions (notices) are given to the CCs. Up to
now, EMO prepares notices (including those prepared for EMO by EEMEP in October 1996 for
starting off the monthly reporting program) and sends these to the XECC Safety Department for
transmittal to the CCs. But it seems that, instead of doing such transmitting, the Safety Department
has assumed the authority to rewrite and sign the notices and has thus watered them down from
precise instructions to virtually meaningless generalities. This in effect makes the Safety Department
responsible for environmental monitoring, but the Safety Department does not have the expertise
needed for this. Responsibility is and should remain with EMO. The Loan 2 Agreement must make it
clear that the role of the Safety Department is simply as a transmission channel, or let EMO
communicate directly with the CCs. The present situation is most unsatisfactory and serves to negate
much of the efforts of EMO/EEMEP.

A "Environmental Management Handbook for XLD Construction" was prepared in English
and in Chinese in Feb. 1995 by EMO/RPDI to be distributed for guiding the activities of the CCs and
others concerned with XLD construction. This is included here as Supplemental Annex 4.2.2.4-1,
which includes detailed discussion of all laws and regulations applicable to the construction
operations. This was updated in Dec. 95 (Supplemental Annex 4.2.2.4-2).
Additional information on these same subjects are given in Supplemental Annexes 4.2.2-1, 2,
and 3 respectively on "Environmental Requirements in Construction Contracts", "Guidelines for
Monthly Environmental Reporting by CCs", and "Applicable Laws and Regulations on Environment
for CCs".

4.2.3 Environmental Review of XP Resettlement
4.2.3.1 Introduction
4.2.3.1.1 Basic Principles
In most major dam and reservoir projects in China and other developing counties, the issue
of involuntary resettlement of displaced people is one of the most difficult and sensitive
environmental issues. This is due to the inherent complexities involved, and to inadequate planning
and implementation efforts traditionally applied to addressing resettlement issues in the past. There
are numerous examples of involuntary resettlement with inadequate planning, funding, and follow-
up, producing unacceptable impacts on the quality of life of both resettled and host population.
4-4



Increasingly effective communication and growing national and international concerns for
environmental and social issues are requiring that all major projects ensure the equitable distribution
of benefits, so that no segment of society benefits at the expense of others. In the Xiaolangdi project,
the resettled population, currently established in areas of generally high fertility are likely to be
disadvantaged unless protected by proper planning, while others living downstream benefit
significantly from improved flood protection, water supply, irrigation, and power.
The first criterion in the Xiaolangdi resettlement program is that the resettled population
should not only maintain its current standard of living but should also share in project benefits.
YRCC, in cooperation with the provincial and central government agencies concerned, is committed
to achieving this objective. The population affected by the project includes the resettlers displaced by
inundating and the population in the areas receiving resettlers (the host population). Both resettlers
and host should thus share in project benefits.
Because of its significance and sensitivity, the resettlement issue is the project's most
complex, difficult, and costly environmental problem to resolve. Accordingly YRCC's approach has
been to plan and implement a RS program which meets the essential criteria noted above.
4.2.3.1.2 Assignment of Responsibilities
The work of planning the RS program was assigned by YRCC to RPDI as reported in
ESA/92, and RPDI has continued in this role. Since establishment of RO in 1994, RPDI's work on
RS has been on behalf of RO. Planning at the local level has been done by RO/RPDI together with
the various PROs (Provincial RS Offices) and CROs (County RS Offices). The construction of the
RS villages including village infrastructure has been carried out mainly by the CROs. Evaluation of
progress in planning, construction, and operation has been carried out by YRCC/RO together with the
North China Institute of Water Conservancy and Hydroelectric Power (NCIWCHP).    The  MWR
Resettlement Office is responsible overall for monitoring resettlement implementation.
The RS program is being carried out in four phases, representing people moved as follows:
(i) Phase I, from construction zone, (ii) Phase II, from reservoir area for elevation below El 180 m,
(iii) Phase III, from reservoir area for El 180 to 265 m, and (iv) Phase IV, from reservoir are for El
265 to 276 m.
A summary of the entire program, as envisioned in 1992 compared to what has happened
since, up to Feb. 1997, is shown in Figure 4.2.3.1.2-1.
4.2.3.2 Resettlement Planing
4.2.3.2.1 Planning Prior to 1994
Because the RS issue is an extremely sensitive issue from the environmental point of view,
YRCC has allocated a very sizable portion of the total XP feasibility study to RS planning. For this
reason, the RS planning report is voluminous. For the purpose of the present report the essential features
of the RS planning/program are briefly described below. Historically YRCC started its RS planning
more then 20 years ago. This resettlement planning has involved 13 counties (county-level cities) in
Henan and Shanxi Provinces, with 8 located in reservoir area, and 5 host counties located adjacent to the
Yellow River, downstream of the damsite. The planning involved the active participation of YRCC and
of the leaders and agencies at the provincial, municipal, and county levels.
Several surveys of the population, land, and structures to be inundated were undertaken by
RPDI in 1959, 1970, 1980, 1982, 1985, and 1989; and the latest revisions were carried out in September
1991. Preliminary resettlement plans were prepared in 1985 and 1989. The 1991 population census
indicates that the total population to be affected by the Xiaolangdi Project is 159,971, composed of
9,799 at the damsite construction area and 150,172 in the area to be inundated (El. 275 m). Of this,
144,759 people have an agricultural registration and 15,212 have non-agricultural registration. The
resettlement was planned to be carried out on a staggered basis, the number of people to be relocated
takes account of population growth from the base year to the year of moving. The directly affected
population to be moved numbers 181,050, of which 163,762 are agricultural and 17,288 non-
agricultural. The farmland to be inundated or affected will be 366 thousand mu, and the area of houses
and caves to be inundated will be 4.862 million m2, 87% located in rural villages and 13% in towns.
4-5



As the majority of the resettlers are registered as agricultural, most will be resettled in
agriculture. Since there is little suitable land that is not already cultivated, "host" areas will share the
existing cultivated land with the newcomers through provision of additional water by irrigation or other
earning opportunities. As far as is possible, resettlers will be resettled in their own county, except where
their number exceeds the capacity of a county to accept the resettlers.
The area to be inundated is essentially a rural agricultural area composed of small groupings of
rural residences, housing 100-400 people each. Several of these residential clusters ("natural villages")
lie within the boundaries of an administrative village. An administrative village is the smallest unit in
the Chinese administrative hierarchy of land use. The next level is the township or "xiang", which
groups together several administrative villages.
4.2.3.2.2 Continuine Planninm
To meet the requirements of Henan and Shanxi provinces, RPDI conducted as survey in March-
December 1994 to obtain updated information on RS progress. According to these data, the XP/RS
program is described as follows:
(a) Area to be Inundated
The inundated area is below level 276 m. The total land area affected by inundation is 278 km2
in Henan and Shanxi provinces including 8 counties, 29 townships, and 174 villages. Some 48% of the
inundated land is fannland, 9% is timber forest, 12% is firewood forest, 16% is hill land, and 15% is for
other purpose usage.
(b) PoDulation Impact
For the survey baseline year (1994), the total population affected by inundation was 175,591
person. By time the dam is completed, the affected people will reach to 188,138, of which 169,640 are
farmers, 11,752 are government employees, 4,948 are enterprise employees, and 1,798 are retired
persons or others.
(c) Housing
The total housing space to be impacted by inundation is 7.43 million m2, of which 6.87 million
m2 (92.5%) is in rural area, 6.7% in township, 0.8% belongs to enterprises. Of the rural housing, 23.9%
is brick house or cave, 3 1.1% is earth cave. Average house space per capita is 37.6 m2.
(d) Industries
Some 787 enterprises will be affected by inundation, of which 69% are mines and 31% are
factories. The total employee number 54,499 person, of which 49,551 are employed by township
enterprises with seasonal contracts. Their salary is the main sources of their family income. These
people expect to be transfenred to be permanent employees by the RS program.
(e) Others
The XP will also have impacts on other aspects including:
schools                                186
agriculture training centers           I
hospitals                              14
epidemic prevention stations           200
entertainment centers                  177
as well as some infrastructures such as, road 1,022 km, cannel 1,652 km, small hydro-power stations
13, pumping stations 587, transmission line (10 kv) 917 km, and communication line 778 km.
The situation on RS Planning shown in Figure 4.2.3.1.2-1 includes planning before and after
1994. As noted above, responsibility for RS planning at the YRCC level was assigned in 1994 to the
new RO in YRWHDC established that year. It also shows the key questions involved in the planning
operation for all four RS phases with scheduling of the planning and construction of the RS investment
components.
4.2.3.2.3 Laws and Eauitv PrinciWles Relating to Resettlement
(a) Laws Relating to RS: The RS planning observed all laws and regulations relating to RS.
These are summarized in Tables 4.2.9.2-1 and 2 of Section 4.2.9.
(b) Equitv Principles: Based on the principle of the laws, regulations and other relevant rules,
and knowledge gained from resettlement experience at completed reservoirs elsewhere in China, 17
4-6



resettlement criteria were developed by YRCC in conjunction with local governments for the
Xiaolangdi Project. These are listed in Figure 4.2.3.2.3-1
4.2.3.2.4 Planning Criteria
Resettlement planning for the Xiaolangdi Project focuses on the siting and construction of new
residential clusters (RS villages) within existing host administrative areas. The new sites include both
housing and adjacent agricultural land as described below.
(a) Basic Agricultural Production Scenarios
There are two basic production models proposed for agricultural resettlement: (i) dry land
(rained) farming, and (ii) partially irrigated farmiing scenario. The minimum per-capita requirement for
household consumption is considered to be 225 kg grain per year, and these are maintained in both
scenarios. In this way, both the resettlers and host population will be self-sufficient in grains, so that
they may go on to grow multiple cash crops or set up other small business activities.
The amount of land requisitioned for the resettlers differs according to conditions in each host
area. For example, to allow for population increases before resettlement takes place, a minimum area of
1.7 mu/resettler is recommended for rain-fed agricultural situations where small business activities,
referred to as "sideline" income, can supplement land based farm income. Where sideline income
possibilities are limited, more land is required to provide the necessary per capita income. For areas
irrigated, 0.8 mu/resettler is the minimum provided.
The basic objective of agricultural resettlement planning is to increase per capita income of
both the resettlers and the host people by 5-15% in real terms compared with that before resettlement.
Considerable amounts of money and land must be provided, and diversification developed, so that the
previous individual income levels are maintained and increased. The overall agricultural planning for
the reservoir area shows that the requisitioned land totals 183,281 mu; the people to be resettled total
153,906; the agricultural jobs total 63,601; and the investment needed totals 670 million yuan. On
average, the cultivated land allocation will be 1.2 mu per resettler and investment 10,534 yuan per
agricultural job.
(b) Non-Agricultuml Production
With the large number of resettlers created by the Xiaolangdi Project, it is not possible for all
resettlers to continue their livelihoods in agriculture. To provide enough jobs for the relocatees and to
increase their incomes, 8,973 resettlers are to work in 8 enterprises. Their planned investment totals 107
million yuan, representing 12,224 yuan per worker. So one out of each 15 resettlers is to resettled in
non-agricultural activities. In addition, it is planned to transfer the agricultural status of 17,210 family
members of government employees relocated into the non-agricultural status. These family members
are to employed by the government unit where their family member works, by the local labour
department, or by private businesses. Investment for this will total 72.74 million yuan. About one out of
each 15 resettlers is to be provided with a non-agricultural job.
(c) Residential Planning for Villages
Currently the rural village housing occupies 14,912 mu, or 0.12 mu per capita. The residences
and cave dwellings have adequate floor space, per capita of 26.7 m2, but the construction quality is
older and the sanitary conditions are poor. The caves account for about 30% of the floor space of
residences.
The new residential areas were selected jointly by the Research Planning and Design Institute
of YRCC, together with the counties, townships (xiangs), and affected villages. A total of 267 host sites
were selected. Some 53,787 people from 12,205 families will be moved downstream to 39 host sites on
the Wenmengtan plain; 55,386 people will move to new xiangs within the same county at 117 host sites;
and 29,886 people will "move-back" within the same xiang to 91 host areas. Most of the selected areas
are located along the boundaries between two or more administrative villages, so that the land sharing
problem between resettlers and hosts villagers is minimized. The new sites provide suitable agricultural
land and adequate drinking water for both people and livestock, roads access, power and
communications, and other community facilities. Based on present water consumption for human and
livestock use, it is calculated that the water use lever is about 60 litres per capita per day. Water supply
sources include groundwater and surface water from small reservoirs.
4 -7



(d) Planning for Towns
The largest affected population centres are referred to as towns, which serve as governnment
service centres for xiangs and large administrative villages. Most of the 11 towns inundated are situated
in valleys or plains where the natural soil conditions are favorable for agriculture, and where transport
facilities are available. These places are the political, economic and cultural centres in the local regions,
and play an important part in the local economy.
Of the 11 towns, 10 towns have average populations of 1-2,000 people and 1 town contains
approximately 5,000 people. The total town population inundated is 24,865 people with 17,413 having
an agricultural status and 7,452 having non-agricultural status. The population of one town is to be
dispersed among the other towns; a second town is to be a xiang seat; and the remaining 9 towns are to
be relocated but will retain their present administrative function.
The average basic town population size is to remain the same, and the floor space per capita for
buildings at the new site is to be the same as for the old site. Infrastructure is being somewhat improved
including such items as streets, power supply, water supply and sewers, etc.
(e) Infrastructure Planning
Infrastructure to be inundated includes roads, transmission lines, communication lines,
broadcasting facilities, factories, mines, water supply works, and other community facilities.
Factories and mines and the special facilities are to be compensated and rebuilt according to
their original standards. Among the 93 factories and mines, 65 are to be transferred to other products, 26
will be rebuilt in new places, and 2 will cease to exist. All RS-sponsored factories and mines are to be
required to comply with PRC laws and regulations on pollution emissions. This is one aspect of
environmental monitoring by EMO, to check on such performance, which will be implemented in 1997.
The arterial highways are to be rebuilt to the same grade with the same quality of materials.
New residential areas are to be serviced with simple highways. Wharves and ferries around the reservoir
are to be rebuilt, enlarged or removed, as each location warrants. Every village is to be serviced by
electrical distribution lines. Telephone lines and broadcasting facilities are also guaranteed to every
village. Water supply, drainage, and solid waste management facilities are also to be provided for every
village.
4.2.3.2.5 Implementation of Resettlement
The resettlement bureaus at the provincial level in Shanxi and Henan Provinces, together with
the CROs, have primary responsibility for the implementation and management of all resettlement
activities. Resettlement offices have been established in the affected counties and county-level cities.
Funds for resettlement are distributed to the two Provincial Resettlement Bureaus directly from the
Construction Bank of China, and then from the province to the local govermment departments of the
host counties and xiangs. All the resettlement activities are monitored by YRWRHDC/RO, including
RO's EMO.
