4.2.3.3 Resettlement Progress
4.2.3.3.1 Phase I (Damsite Construction Zone Resettlement)
The land requisitioned for the damsite construction area includes land occupied by the main
works of the dam project, borrow areas, disposal areas, work areas, and camp areas for housing
construction personnel. Investigations carried out in 1990 show that the construction sites cover a total
area of 33,500 mu of which 20,083 mu had been farmland. A total population of 9,798 people are
affected as a result of the construction of the project. The resettlement planning shows that 75% of the
total 9,978 resettlers from the damsite construction zone remained in agriculture and 25% went to non-
agricultural employment. Roughly one of each 2 labor forces went to non-agricultural activities.
Figure 4.2.3.3.1- 1 shows the host site for XP/RS Phase I.
4.2.3.3.2 Phase 11 Resettlement Program
Phase II resettlement from the reservoir area (below El 180 m) involves a total population of
46,000 in 27 administration villages, including 42000 rural people. In the Phase II resettlement
planning, it was previously planned to provide 45 new sites, but this total number has been reduced to
34 through optimization during implementation. House construction for 25,000 people was completed
in 1995 as scheduled. The work quantities completed in 1996 included new houses for 17,000 people
plus corresponding infrastructures. Out of the total resettlement population, 4,000 people are being
relocated together with industries and institutions. By the end of 1996 settlement has been completed for
11,268 families (about 40,000 people), including 6,000 mu land for new sites, 1.011 million m2 houses,
35,1596 mu land for production use, 36 wells, 97 km high-voltage power lines, 45 substations, and 65
km xiang-village roads. The total number of moved families is 4,478, together 16,580 people. The sites
for Phase II RS villages are shown in Figure 4.2.3.3.2- 1.
4.2.3.3.3 Phase III
Phase III resettlement from the reservoir area (El 180-265 m) has been initiated. The completed
work quantities will include 18,100 mu land for production use, 4 pump stations, 9 new enterprises, new
housing for 456 families, and 41.4 km roads. Construction of basic facilities for Gucheng Township has
been essentially completed, including land acquisition, water/power supply provision, and road
construction. A total of 16,100 m2 buildings have been completed for the township government.
River training and warping works completed at Wenmengtan in December 1996 include:
4-9



(a) Land accretion: 10.62 million m3 soil works, accounting for 85% of the total design
quantity (12.54 million m3);
(b) Warping: 1.66 million mu, taking up 25% of the total design quantities (6.44 million mu);
(c) Training works: 63 spurs, or 53% of the total design quantities (118);
(d) Dyke: 150,000 m3.
Regarding Houhe reservoir and irrigation area project, the reservoir design has been reviewed
but not yet approved by MWR. The preparatory works include provision of access roads, buildings,
water/power supply facilities, telecommunications, and land for construction use. Tendering for the
main works of the dam component was started December 5, 1996.
Optimization of the irrigation area component has been completed, and jointly appraised by the
MWR River Conservancy Engineering Consultative Center and the China International Engineering
Consultative Company on December 27, 1996.
4.2.3.3.4 Phase IV
Phase IV resettlement refers to people move from elevations up to 275 m, the reservoir level
when the reservoir is full. At 265-275 m, the affected population will be 23,000 person (17,000 in
Henan, 6,000 in Shanxi), with 95,000 mu of affected land (17,000 in Henan, 30,000 in Shanxi). Phase
IV planning is to be finished in 1997 and construction completed in 2010-2015.
4.2.3.3.5 Combining Phases m and IV
Consideration is being given at the present time (Feb. 1997) to combine Phases m and IV into a
single phase, with overall planning to be completed in 1997.
4.2.3.4 Review of Resettlement Ooeration by RS ALencies
4.2.3.4.1 Effects on RS Families
(a) Economic Effects
Figure 4.2.3.4.1-1 has been prepared to summarize the compensation provided for the 18
various types of RS population involved including (i) compensation costs for housing/yard/amenities (in
favor of replacement of these), and (ii) compensation for lost income and compensation for any training
needs and including compensation of income level before and after resettlement. These data indicate
that the RS families will not suffer economic losses because of XLD Project. However, with respect to
agricultural resettlement, the RS report notes the need for continuing work and attention to some
outstanding issues, as follows:
(i) Farn incomes constitute less than half the household incomes of both the affected
population and the hosts. The projected farm incomes of relocatees are unlikely to be much
higher than current levels, although there is some scope to increase incomes by diversifying
cropping patterns, if grain quota requirements are waived. Therefore, agriculture is an unlikely
source to improve living standards.
(ii) Non-farm income sources deserve at least the same level of study and planning to ensure
that rural resettlers living standards do not decline. The Wenmengtan Scheme, for example, has
agricultural development costs of 5,500 yuan per capita or 24,200 yuan per household.
Altemative non-agricultural employment opportunities could be explored at this level of
investment per capita.
(iii) The successful relocation of rural people from the three most affected counties (Xin'an,
Jiyuan and Yuanqu) is dependent on timely completion of the Wenmengtan and Houhe
schemes. Early construction of these projects is important.
(iv) Counties should be careful not to overestimate potential crop yields, since this will tend
to reduce the areas of land allocated to relocatees. Similarly, variations in the quality of land
should also be taken into account.
(v) Early action is required to complete agreements between resettler and host villages. More
staff and resources are required at the County level to undertake this, and other planning tasks at
the village level.
(vi) Serious consideration must be given to introducing preferential policies for rural
relocatees and hosts. This matter is already under consideration and includes, among others,
special status for scarce farm inputs, direct sales of farm products, training,
4 -10



exemption/reduction of grain quotas, priority for jobs in rural enterprises, subsidies and tax
exemptions.
(b) Housine/Amenities/Public Services
Table 4.2.3.4.1-1 summarizes the before and after situation on housing/ amenities and public
services for the various type of RS populations. These data indicate the RS farnilies will generally be
better off than before moving. Figures 4.2.3.4.1-2 to 5 illustrate the typical before and after situation on
housing. It is of interest to note that 60% of the floor space of existing housing of the resettlers (total
population 181,050) is in caves, and that such caves are characteristically damp and commonly
inadequately ventilated.
(c) Social/Cultural Impacts
Because virtually all of the RS and host families are Han Chinese, there should be no ethnic
conflict problems. The social/cultural summary made by the RS team have indicted that most RS
families are aware that they will have to move, but few have any firm concept of the specifics to be
involved. As would be expected, the minority do not much like the idea of moving, but, if required to
move, they would prefer not to be separated from their villager neighbors. However, their main concern
is that the move would hopefully result in an improved standard of living, especially if given the
opportunity to switch from agricultural to non-agricultural livelihoods.
4.2.3.4.2 Effects on Hosts
(a) Host Family Economics
Table 4.2.3.4.2-1 summarizes the information on economic impacts on host families.
(b) Host Community Infrastructure/Public Services
Table 4.2.3.4.2-2 shows the situation on community infrastructure/public services, namely to
show that the RS program will supplement the existing infrastructure/services to accommodate the
increased loading without loss in level of service.
(c) Socio-Cultural Impacts
The RS task surveys indicate that about 70% of the host population is aware of the proposed RS
program; the majority of these believe that the RS program will have significant effects on their
livelihoods; and about two-thirds seem willing to accommodate the RS families. The indication is that
the "proof of the pudding will be in the eating ", that is, the socio-cultural problems will be acceptable
provided that the host villagers are not made worse off economically by the RS program.
4.2.3.4.3 Grievances/Complaints
(a) Mechanics for Grievances/Complaints
The Village Committees, which represent the villagers independently of the regular government
structure, are expected to be the main mechanics for receiving grievances/complaints from RS families
or host families. The village committees, if unable to resolve the problems, will refer them to the local
government and then to higher government levels as needed up to the provincial levels. Grievances are
expected to be of three types, (i) psychological/socio-cultural, (ii) physiological/services, and (iii)
economics. Table 4.2.3.4.3-1 summarizes the avenues for resolution of grievances.
Experience in using the EIA process in various provinces of China has shown that the Village
Committees (also called Residential Community Associations) have been quite effective in gaining
support from the local and provincial governmental agencies, including the Environmental Protection
Bureaus, for solving unfair treatment of the villagers/residents (See "Evaluation of EIA in PRC, Asian
Development Bank, 1991).
(b) Safety Nets
If resettlers or hosts are unsuccessful in achieving satisfactory income levels, they will have
recourse to a special Reservoir Area Support Fund which guarantees minimum incomes should farm or
non-farm livelihoods fail to meet their minimum needs. Applicants to the Fund will be referred through
their village leader to the CRO. This Fund will remain in place until the MWR deems that the
Xiaolangdi relocatees and affected hosts are no longer at risk. This Fund will also support entire villages
should that be necessary. The Ministry of Water Resources' "Reservoir Area Support Fund" will
underwrite the liabilities of the income protection plan.
4- 11



For non-farm workers, employment and income risks will be covered by their units. Where
units go bankrupt, the agreements signed in advance for resettlers to be compensated or integrated into
other units.
4.2.3.4.4 Trans2ort Capacity
The early experience in managing RS for construction camp resettlers indicated some problems
in furnishing adequate transport capacity for moving RS families from existing to new sites. Table
4.2.3.4.4-1 has been prepared to show the updated RS plan for managing this problem.
4.2.3.4.5 Monitoring Provisions in Resettlement Plan
The construction of all Xiaolangdi Project components requires proper supervision and
monitoring. The resettlement component is thus being implemented to ensure the quality and schedule
of resettlement programs, to control costs, to ensure cost effectiveness, and to augment economic
benefits for resettlers and hosts. Special forms are completed by resettlement personnel at xiang or
village level, and submitted to provincial and municipal resettlement bureaus. Copies of these are
submitted to the YRCC Resettlement Office by the CRO, and then enclosed in the construction progress
reports submitted by supervising engineers. Only in this way can resettlement operations be monitored,
and problems identified and quickly resolved. These forms and corresponding written materials are
submitted on a quarterly basis.
YRCC has engaged qualified social institute scientist experts from Hehai University and
NCIWCHP to conduct independent evaluations of socio-economic progress of the resettlers. This
service aims at evaluating and reporting on the level of living conditions and social services of the
affected population, both before and after resettlement. The evaluation are being done every year and
cover (i) farm yields, employment, education, public healt, and incomes in new host areas, (ii) ideas of
the resettlers on organizations, services, and social progress in new sites, and (iii) samples of
inforrnation on effects on host comununities. Socio-economic evaluations are based on a number of key
indices for selected households. The budget for RS monitoring is estimated to be Yuan 14 million, and
will be financed from the resettlement compensation budget The monitoring program described above
is reviewed periodically by the EMO, to ensure that it is proceeding satisfactorily. This is explained in
Section 5 of this report (See Section 5 of the present report).
4.2.3.4.6 Infrastructure and Industry
(a) Infrastructure
The RS infrastructure program has been planned to ensure that the various public services to be
furnished in the new communities and host communities will give levels of service to both RS families
and affected host families at least as good as previously, and so that host communities will be furnished
with funding to finance needed public service improvements.
Some questions have been raised about the economic viability of some of the infrastructure
aspects of the overall RS program including the Houhe reservoir schemes, the Xiping reservoir scheme,
and terracing of steep hillsides. Each of these is being further evaluated, but all are relatively minor
aspects of the overall RS plan and these questions will hardly affect the soundness of the overall
economic RS plan evaluation. Questions have been raised also about the reliability of the land
reclamation schemes along the river below the dam. In this connection, the YRCC Design Institute is
believed to have the needed expertise in this field of work. The land reclamation program is already
well underway (being financed separately from the XLD Project).
(b) Industries
One of the RS problems involves industries which are established to create jobs for resettlers,
i.e., are these industries in compliance with environmental laws and regulations, particularly with
respect to management of waste emissions (liquid, solid, gaseous)? Part of the ERD's program is to
ensure that industries established for RS purposes do comply with the laws and regulations. This then is
another assignment for the ERDIRPDI field inspector team.
4.2.3.4.7 Natural Resources and Archeological Relics
Field visits to the various RS sites have not indicated problems of adverse effects on existing
forests, wildlife, or other natural resources. This is because the areas involved are already "people-ized".
4 -12



The field visits and consultation with archeological experts have not found evidence of any
archeological relics which would be effected by the RS plan.
4.23.4.8 Aereements between RS Program Participants
A critical aspect of the overall RS program is timely procurement of written agreements
between the various agencies participating in the program, from highest to lowest levels. This program
is underway as discussed in Section 4.2.3.4-3.
4.2.3.4.9 Overview and Risks
The situation on risks may be summarized as follows:
(a) Resettlement Planning: All general planning activities have been completed, but some
improvements are needed as follows:
(i)   Develop detailed implementation plans and schedules for RS program for Phases III and
IV (for El 180-276 m).
(ii) Increase the level of public consultation and integration with the planning decision
process.
(b) Resettlement Feasibility
(i)  Implementation of RS Phase I for the damsite construction zone is already completed.
Income situation of resettlers is being investigated and monitored by CRO and institutes.
(ii) Resettlement Implementation Planning for phase II (below El 180) is completed, and
moving of the resettlers will be completed in October, 1997.
(iii) Resettlement Feasibility Planning for Phase III and IV (El 180 to El 276) is completed,
and detailed village planning is underway.
(iv) Xinan recommended a resettlement scheme in which 20% relocatees were to be resettled
within the county and 80% moved to Wenmengtan and the three downstream counties. This
scheme is feasible technically, socially, and economically. However, its successful
implementation requires that YRCC fumish the river training works ready ahead of schedule,
that provincial government properly coordinates the socio-economic relations between
resettlers and host counties, and that county resettlement offices undertake detailed work among
both resettlement and host communities.
(v)  According to Jiyuan resettlement scheme, 70% resettlers will be moved near or back
within the county, and 30% will be moved to Wenmengtan. Apart from this, a potential exists
for industrial and agricultural resettlement. The scheme is technically, socially, and
economically feasible.
(vi) Yuanqu proposes to resettle all the resettlers within its boundary, and most will go to the
Houhe Irrigation Zone. As listed in the capital construction plan of the MWR, this reservoir is
ensured for investments. So success of the scheme depends upon the execution of the reservoir
and development of its benefits as planned. Because the feasibility design indicates the project
benefits will be realized as intended, the resettlement scheme is considered to be feasible from
the technical, social, and economic point of view.
(vii) With more farmland from which more land can be shared by relocatees, Mianchi and
Mengjin provide land for all resettlers.
(viii) With less than 1% of their county total populations affected, Shanxian, Pinglu, and
Xiaxian will arrange for their resettlers intemally by moving back within the same xiang or
village.
(c) Risks
The following resettlement risks have been identified and should be considered during
resettlement planning and implementation decisions:
(i)   Timing of land development of Wenrnengtan should be further considered after the dam
project is completed. Certain schedules for downstream works have to be further determined,
and local governments need to arrange boundaries between shared land. It is critically
significant to make sure that this site will be adequate to support resettlement El 180 to 265.
4- 13



(ii) Local government funding may not be available in time for investing in industrial
enterprises. Whether these enterprises can be made available in time will affect resettlers
employment and economic incomes.
(iii) The downstream RS Planning for Kaifeng, Zhongmu, and Yuanyang proposes to utilize
marginal lands that would be brought up to agronomic standards through deposition of river silt.
While the technical and economic rationale of this land improvement technique has been
established in other situations, the social acceptability of relocating up to 200 km distance away
from the reservoir needs further evaluation.
(iv) At present, fifty percent or more of household income may come from non-agricultural
sources. The ability of proposed non-agricultural employment to sustain this income level must
still be demonstrated.
(v)  If the resettlement cost estimate is found to be too low during implementation (on
account of quantities, unit values, or compensation funds for purchasing power), the cost
estimate will be required to be readjusted quickly. If the government cannot guarantee
resettlement financing, project activities will have to be suspended.
(vi) Offsetting the many negative risks is a positive one: A high proportion ( in excess of 50
percent ) of the resettlement task is carried out by local manpower. This manpower is available,
and is priced low.
4.2.3.4.10 Compliance with Environmental Laws
This subject is discussed in Section 4.2.3.2.3 (a).
4.2.3.5 Summary Environmental Evaluation
(a) Discussion
The overall RS project accomplishments to date are believed to represent a remarkable effort
for planning and implementing developing an effective RS program for a major dam/reservoir project in
China, a truly pioneering effort involving a multitude of details and complexities. Considering that the
RS planning and preparations seem well underway and in hand, and assuming support from YRCC and
the other governmental agency participants will continue to be furnished as planned, it is concluded that
the overall RS program is quite well in hand. Nothing of this complexity will ever be perfect, but it is
concluded that the RS planning progress and future expectations will meet EIA requirements of both
government and WB provided that the recommended improvements made by the Environmental
Engineer Member and Sociologist Member of the Expert Panel on Environment and Resettlement will
be duly implemented. These recommendations may be briefly summarized as follows:
(al) Environmental Enaineeriny Asnects of RS: This will require implementation of the
EEMEP's recommendation that the operations of the ERD/RPDI field environmental inspector team be
expanded to give "equal attention" to RS as for the dam construction operation, and the field visits made
by the EEMEP during Expert Panel meetings will allow sufficient time for Panel surveillance of the
facilities being furnished. This includes attention to environmental conditions at factories especially oi1
waste emissions, as discussed in Item (d) below.
(a2) Sociologist Asvects of RS: This requires implementation of the recommendation of the
Sociologist Member of the Expert Panel to allow more time during Panel field visits for direct contacts
with resettlement families who have moved and lived in their new quarters for significant periods, and
that the RS/MIS system be effectively implemented by RO, including recommendations made by the
Sociologist Panel Member.
(b) Evaluation
Figure 4.2.3.5-1 summarizes the Environmental Evaluation of the RS program to date. This
information confirms that the YRWHDC/RO has indeed done a remarkable job, in solving the
numerous difficult problems involved in a complex pioneering program of this type, in achieving the
primary goal of getting the RS village housing, housing amenities, and village infrastructure built on
time and at good quality levels, and in arranging for moving and for new working arrangements so the
RS families and hosts are not economically disadvantaged but actually do gain from the RS operations.
The only tiing lacking has been sufficient monitoring and surveillance to ensure that the physical
facilities built are built to acceptable standards and will be corrected when needed and will be operated
4 - 14



and maintained so that the investment in building these facilities will actually realize the planned
benefits for the health and welfare of the RS families and villages. Moreover the recommended
monitoring/surveillance program will cost only a tiny fraction of the investment cost for building the
facilities.
(c) Recommendations for Phvsical Facilities of RS Villages
The recommended monitoring/surveillance program comprises the following components in the
physical facilities program:
(i)   Expansion of the ERD/RPDI field operations to include visits to each village every 3
months, to check on adequacy of facilities built and of the status of O&M.
(ii)  Appointment of a "Working Level Environmental Officer" at each RS village, with
suitable pay, who will ensure that O&M is satisfactory and who will keep adequate records on
this, for all pertinent environmental issues.
(iii) Visits by the EEMEP to selected RS villages every six months to check on the above.
For example, on (ii), the most important single need is for chlorine residual testing at a number
of home water taps on a weekly basis, with a written record of these, to ensure safety of the
water supply for drinking. If there were to be a waterborne disease epidemic at any RS village,
the absence of such records would per se represent an indictment of performance of all
responsible parties including the Bank. Another example, the EEMEP's visits indicate lack of
attention to keeping the excellent village storm drainage channels from getting filled with trash,
hence if a serious storm occurs the system will not furnish the intended protection. Again, some
continuing guidance is needed to ensure that the RS village sanitary landfills are actually
operated in a sanitary manner, to avoid serious public health hazards from insect and rodent
disease vectors. Again, some hand washing facilities are needed in the toilet rooms of schools
where dry toilet systems are used.
In addition to the above, some periodic technical guidance is needed during the
construction phase for RS facilities for RS facilities to ensure that the facilities as built are
adequate. Generally they are but sometimes some technical guidance input is needed, for
example, to determine whether the mineral quality of well water supplies is adequate when it
appears if nay not be, due to color, iron, excessive dissolved solids, etc. In addition periodic
checking is needed on pollution control performance of factories built for RS purposes.
(d) Summary
To sum up, it appear that an excellent job is being generally done in provision of the physical
facilities, and all that is needed to "perfect" the overall program is an appropriate monitoring and
surveillance program (which involves only a tiny fraction of the total investment cost). This will require
major upgrading of the environmental monitoring as practical up to now.
4.2.4   Archeological Relics
4.2.4.1 Relics Protection Plan
As noted in Section 3.2.13 of Chapter 3, the XLD reservoir area is within the region where
the Chinese Han peoples originated, hence the area contains numerous archeological relics,
especially in the main population/farning areas of the middle and lower Yellow River reaches.
Although the density of cultural relics is much lower in the gorge area where the dam and reservoir
will be located, a detailed archaeological exploration, classification, and salvage program has been
undertaken to locate, recover, and protect important underground and surface relics and cultural
treasures in the area to be inundated. This program is delineated in Figure 4.2.4-1. This program is
summarized in Table 4.2.4.3-1. It is in compliance with the Archeological Law of China, which
requires that all important relics are to be excavated, protected, and where appropriate, preserved or
reconstructed.
4.2.4.2 Relics Program
(a) Underground relics program: It includes two stages: (a.l) Drilling, excavation, and
salvage; (a.2) clearing.
(a. 1) Drilling, excavation, and salvage: The underground archeological relics in XLD
project reservoir zone are distributed in about 5.34 million m2. The HSAIs decided that 80-100% of
4 - 15



the area is to be drilled, and 10-15% be excavated. After excavation, the objects with values like
earthware, stoneware, etc. will be repaired and stored in museums. For the rest, like tomb, ash pit,
etc., these will be recorded in writing and photos for further study.
(a.2) Clearing: Those with less value and/or are seriously damaged will not be stored and
studied further. These relics will be selected on the basis of estimated value.
(b) Relics above ground: According to the evaluation, the main components of those with
historical values will be removed and relocated, if possible, and those which cannot be removed will
be sampled and recorded.
4.2.4.3 Program Costs
The overall budget for the archaeological protection plan was 17.20 million yuan on the base
price of 1991, including exploration boring, excavation, temporary storage, repair, relocation, and
reconstruction. After the meeting (presided by MWR) of December 1995 for planning the relics
protection program, the HSAIs re-categorized the cultural relics and historical sites in the
construction and reservoir areas into three categories: major importance, relatively importance, and
general. Considering the price inflation (based on 1994), the original cost for relics program was
adjusted to 22.50 million yuan. Also, an extra investment of 4.87 million yuan was found to be
required by the two provincial relics bureaus to salvage the two most important relics, Xiwo Grottos
of the North Wei Dynasty in Henan Province and Yuanqu culture remains of the Shang Dynasty in
Shanxi Province. The Xiwo Grottos will be relocated and reconstructed, which costs about 3.00
million yuan. The Yuanqu historical site will be unearthed completely, which will cost 1.87 million
yuan. Table 4.2.4.3-1 shows the yearly investment in the relics program.
4.2.4.4 Program Proeress
At the meeting in Dec. 1995, an understanding was reached on needs for to the effect of
archeological protection in the XLD reservoir area between the provincial archeological agencies,
related departments, and experts. Subsequently the HSAIs, entrusted by YRWHDCRO, undertook
investigation and evaluation of cultural relics and historical sites in the construction and reservoir
areas. In the reservoir area there are altogether 109 cultural relics and historical sites below El 275 m,
including 80 from the New Stone Age to the Tang Dynasty period. In Shanxi Province, 3 above-
ground ancient structures and 55 stales from the Ming and Tang Dynasties have been discovered. The
relics discovered in Henan Province include Xiwo Grottos of the North Wei Dynasty, the Emperor
Tang Temple of the Yuan Dynasty, as well as other ancient structures and carved stones. All these
are valuable for architectural and artistic studies of ancient China. They will be excavated and
salvaged at different times prior to creation of the reservoir.
Some examples to illustrate progress on the relics program are given here:
(a) Xiwo stone cave: It is located at Xiwo township, south bank of east Yellow River, 40 km
from Xinan county. The Xiwo Grottos are located on the cliff of the south bank of the Yellow River,
8 m to the upper road and 10 m to the river edge. The actual elevation of the Grottos is 168 m, which
is 12 m lower than the water level in the reservoir (180 m) in first stage. The Grottos include 171
niches, 4 Buddha towers, and about 200 statues. It is about 1,400 year old. It is the only relic which is
classified at the "Provincial Protection Level" in the inundated area. It will be the first time in China
to relocate and reconstruct a stone cave as a whole. A series of procedures including evaluation,
historic change, collection of related informnation, investigation of environmental problems such as
presence of un-steady surrounding rock, etc. has been set and approved by experts and government
agencies. The new location for the Grottos, Qiantangzhizhai museum in Xinan county, has been
selected. Cutting and moving the Grottos will be completed by the end of 1997, and reconstruction
will be carried out in 1998. Photos of the Grottos are attached (See Figure 4.2.4.4-1).
(b) Zhouli site: It is a historical site of Longshan early age, located at Zhouli village, Mengjin
county in Henan Province. It is one of the ten important archaeological relics classified as the "China
State Protection Level". Some 12 house foundations, 120 ash pits, and 16 tombs have been
excavated, as well as some potterie, stonewares, etc. It is valuable to study the Chinese origins.
Figure 4.2.4.4-2 shows some photos of Zhouli site.
