4.2.5   Dam Stability against Earthauakes
4.2.5.1 Analysis of Dam Stability against Earthquakes
The Xiaolangdi Project will result in a deep and relatively large reservoir whose mass may
result in induced earthquakes in or near the reservoir area. Of the 200 large reservoirs built around the
world, four have caused earthquakes with an intensity of class 5.5 or more on the Richter scale.
Because the dam is located in a region with a history of serious earthquakes, and there are large faults
passing through the dam/reservoir site, the issue of dam safety is very important.
YRCC has attached great importance to dam stability and has carried out extensive program of
studies on this subject as applied to XP. Between 1978 and 1983, geologic mapping and a systematic
survey of the reservoir area produced the first estimates of the distribution, length, and attitude of active
faults in the area. A thorough review of reservoir induced earthquakes within China and abroad was
carried out in 1984 and 1985. Based on this initial work, a detailed study of the probability of
reservoir-induced earthquakes subsequent to the construction of Xiaolangdi was carried out from 1986
to 1987. This included extensive and detailed field studies required to assess the active Quaternary
faults in the reservoir area.   Techniques included   stripping and testing of samples by
thermoluminescence and electron spin resonance methods to furnish the dates of the most recent
activity. Finally detailed studies were made of the Xinfengjiang, Danjiang, Longyangxia, Senwo,
Wuxijiang, Nanchong, and Shengjiaxia reservoirs, which have triggered earthquakes after
impoundment. Based on these investigations and studies, the potential magnitude of induced
earthquakes subsequent to flooding of the Xiaolangdi reservoir is estimated to be between 5.5 and 5.6
on the Richter scale. A magnitude of 5.6 was recommended for design purposes in the document
entitled "Special Report on Reservoir Induced Earthquake Study of Xiaolangdi Project" (February
1988).
During the project appraisal for Phase I loan, both the Panel of Experts on XLD planning and
YRWHHDC attached great importance to the possibility of reservoir-induced earthquakes. Based on the
Panel's recommendations, the seismic stability of the dam, embankments, and the intake towers have
been tested for a simulated magnitude of 6.25 at corresponding horizontal peak acceleration of 0.5 g.
The results indicate that the dam, embankment, and the intake towers would not be endangered under
these conditions. In conclusion, the present seismic design of the dam is considered to be fully
consistent with the best state of the scientific knowledge on this aspect.
4.2.5.2 General Design of Seicmoscopic Network
(a) Need for Network
As above stated, the XLD reservoir is located in a region with a history of frequent serious
earthquakes. The reservoir area is subject to a number of Quaternary faults with the possibility of
reservoir seepage. As a result, there is probability of induced earthquakes. As compared with natural
earthquakes, induced ones have such characteristics as higher frequency, shallower focus, and higher
epicenter intensity. Generally an earthquake less than class 1 is sensible, and an earthquake of class 3
may cause damages. The XLD is a typical high dam with a large reservoir, with all the 16 flood release,
sediment discharge, power generation, and irrigation tunnels arranged on the left bank. The project also
includes a large span powerhouse. In particular, the dam, the deep foundation overburden, and the 100
m high intake tower are sensitive to earthquakes. Therefore, it very necessary to have a special
seismoscopic network for carrying out seismic monitoring, allowing information feedback, probing the
4- 17



rules of seismic occurrence, and providing proper earthquake prediction for the sake of safe construction
and operation of the project and also safety of the people living in and below the damsite area.
(b) General Design
(b.1) Lavout
The XLD seismic monitoring network consists of 9 seismoscopic stations, 1 repeater center,
and I data processing center. The stations are respectively distributed in Mengjin, Xinan, and Jiyuan,
while the processing center is located in Luoyang. Out of the 9 stations, the Donggou, Qiaoling,
Shangmengzhuang, Dangyao, and Nanguanlangtai stations are single directional, with the others located
at Lang, Pangxiejiao, Qingshigeta, and the low-gain active line station at the processing center. As
illustrated in Figure 4.2.5-1, the station at Lang and the active-line station are located upstream of the
dam, but all the others are located downstream.
(b.2) Establishment of Network and Freguencv Response
The network is established using telemetry module-module antenna transmission, visible and
magnetic-medium recording, man-machine interaction, and computer real-time data processing. The
frequency response adopted for the network is displacement flat-type response in which the high-gain
telemetry station is designed to have vertical speed flat-type response in addition to displacement flat-
type response. Displacement flat-type response is also built into the three-directional low gain station
located at the data processing center, allowing complete recording of any earthquake at ML>3.5 within
the network and the inner edge.
(b.3) Recordin2z and Data Processing
Recording is designed to be accomplished with visible analog recording and magnetic-medium
digital recording with seismic event trigger. The latter is used as the principal data recording approach,
with all seismic events (especially those at ML=1.5) transmitted by different stations recorded in
magnetic media in the digital format. The digital sampling rate is 200 times/s.
The data processing system comprises a real-time monitoring data processing subsystem and an
off-line processing subsystem. The former is responsible for identifying and testing seismic event,
calculating epicenter, magnitude, time and focus depth of earthquake events, and subsequently giving
preliminary analysis results. Also, it keeps digital records on permanent and reusable floppy disks. The
latter carries out further data processing of seismic data by man-machine interaction, and also provides
seismic distance, spectrum and focus mechanisms as routine parameters. Any seismic event occurring in
the specified range is reported to responsible departments as early as possible, and a database is
established for the submission of ten-day reports and seismological observation reports. In addition,
such data is also utilized to carry out special studies on seismicity, focus mechanism, induced
earthquake, and damsite stress status.
4.2.5.3 Network Construction and Operation
As required in the general design, the network construction was started in December 1994, with
equipment installation and debugging completed by October 1995. The overall network was put into
operation in November 1995. The service area is focused on the area 40 km upstream and 8 km
downstream, covering a total land area of 1,400 km2. Within this range, any seismic event at ML=0.5
will not be missed, and the epicenter positioning accuracy can be controlled at 0.5 km.
The network was formally put into operation on January 1, 1995. After the checkout operation
from March 1 to May 31, 1996, the network was put into normal operation on June 1, 1996. The results
from the 3-month checkout period included 2,470 pages of medium record printouts, 1,142 magnetic
medium sampling events, 104 floppy disks, 15 processed seismic events, and 3 monthly reports.
According to statistical data, the signal continuity rate is 99.31%, providing an overall score of 98.84
points. During the period starting November 1, 1995 and ending May 31, 1996, a total of 26 seismic
events were measured in the network, the edge, and the vicinity. The positioning results of earthquakes
within the network and its edge are generally consistent with the national catalogue. A ML 6.4
earthquake occurring in Baotou City, Inner Mongolia was recorded by the low-gain station on May 3,
1996. The XLD network was reviewed and accepted by responsible authorities and specialists in July
1996. Altogether 23 earthquakes (including 18 in the network and its edge) were processed during the
recent normal operation period from June 1 to December 31, 1996, resulting in 8,578-page printouts,
4 - 18



more than 1,000 magnetic medium sampling events, 23 disks, 95.29% signal continuity rate, and 7
monthly reports.
The above mentioned operation practice indicates stable and reliable operation results from the
rational design of the network, thus completely satisfying the XP earthquake warning requirements.
4.2.6  Public Health
4.2.6.1 Situation in ESA
The Environmental Study Area has a number of serious public health concerns, especially
communicable diseases, which pose significant problems if protective measures are not undertaken.
A summary of the existing situation with respect to communicable diseases in the Xiaolangdi
Reservoir vicinity is given in Figure 3.2.12-1 of Chapter 3. The prevalent communicable diseases
common in the study area include (a) malaria, (b) encephalitis, (c) hemorrhagic fever, (d) brucellosis,
(e) paragonimiasis, and (f) sanitation-related enteric diseases including dysentery, hepatitis, and
typhoid. The Xiaolangdi Project could significantly increase the hazards of malaria, encephalitis,
hemorrhagic fever, and enteric diseases.
4.2.6.2 Health Effects of XP
The XP will markedly change the hydrology of the Yellow River by creation of a reservoir,
and by providing an opportunity for increased irrigation of the surrounding and downstream
agricultural areas. These changes may increase the hazards for vector-borne diseases such as malaria
and encephalitis, and for communicable diseases such as dysentery, hepatitis, and typhoid. This is
particularly true for the area around the reservoir, and newly irrigated agricultural lands. Inundation
of the reservoir area will also force mass out-migration of disease-bearing rats, almost certainly
resulting in an increased hazard of hemorrhagic fever in the surrounding area. Furthermore, the influx
of construction workers will increase the risk of both vector-borne and sanitation-related enteric
diseases in the vicinity if the construction areas. It is essential therefore to ensure that public health
programs, including vector control and sanitation, are well planned, adequately funded, and
effectively implemented. The public health vector control and sanitation programs to be implemented
by XP, for controlling the with-project hazards which would not exist without the Project, are
summarized in Figures 4.2.6.2-1. The cost estimates for XP diseases control program are shown in
Figure 4.2.6.2-2. Details are described below.
4.2.6.3 Vector Disease Control in Construction Zone
4.2.6.3.1 Medical Screening/Examinations of Workers
As required by the construction contracts, before entering into the construction sites all the
foreign and Chinese staff have to submit their health certificates and are physically examined on the
yearly basis beginning with commencement of the main works. As entrusted by YRWHDC, Luoyang
Anti-epidemic Bureau conducted these examinations for the construction workers prior to their
mobilization at the Site in 1994. On the basis of the examination results, some disqualified workers
were replaced and rejected. However, no communicable disease cases were found. A total number of
930 workers from Lot III received physical examination in 1994, with no communicable diseases
being detected excepting a few enteric disease cases in the summer. No unusual diseases were found
amnong Lot II workers included in the examination. As for Lot III, 296 workers were covered in the
examination, 7 were found suffering from hepatitis B and were all rejected. A considerable number
of cases of enteric disease were found but no cases were reported in YRWHDC Hospital. Three cases
of encephalitis were found in 1996 and provided with prompt treatment. Table 4.2.6.3.1-1 shows the
medical screening schedule and results in construction camps for 1994 to 1996.
A health safety network has been established in the construction area. Each of the three CCs has
established its own clinic. The YRWHDC has also set up its own hospital of considerable size. In the
event of any disease cases beyond the capability of these facilities, access is available to Luoyang city
hospitals. The safety network at the Site is as illustrated in Figure 4.2.6.3.1 -1.
4.2.6.3.2 Malaria and Encephalitis
The measures for control of mosquito vector diseases in the construction zone are the
following:
4- 19



(a)  Medical screening of all workers at time of employment, and annual medical
examinations of all personnel, carried out by the Construction Contractors (See Table 4.2.6.3. 1-1).
(b) Periodic spraying of all buildings in construction work areas and camps:
(b.1) To prevent insect-borne diseases, mosquito/fly density monitoring and killing activities
have been conducted in all camps and work areas at the Site by Yellow River Central Hospital (YRCH)
and Henan Anti-epidemic Station since 1995. Insecticides made in Germany and Britain were sprayed
indoors, especially inner walls, lobbies, toilets, and rubbish heaps, and quick-acting compound
insecticides were applied outdoors, in particular gardens, places overgrown with grass and weeds,
sewage ditches, drainage ditches, vicinity of dinning halls, open rubbish heaps, and any other external
habitats. Table 4.2.6.3.2-1 shows the activities of monitoring and killing of mosquito larvae in the
construction area in 1995-96. However, this work is done only once per year.
(b.2) The EEMEP's visits to the construction sites in October 1996 showed that the buildings
in these sites were not being sprayed periodically to control mosquito biting, apparently because
YRCH/Anti-Epidemic Stations have not recognized this need. However, this is an important prevention
measure for control of malaria and encephilitis, and one of the present report's recommendations is that
the CCs must do such spraying, sufficiently to control mosquito biting for persons in the buildings, for
example, as least monthly using a persistent pesticide. This need is discussed in Sections 4.2.2 and 7.2.
In summary, to date the data indicate that XP has not resulted in any increase in malaria or
encephalitis incidence in the areas in the construction camps.
4.2.6.3.3 Hemorrhagic Fever
The hemorrhagic fever control program is based on (a) control of density of rats (including
mice) in the control areas, as shown in Figure 4.2.6.3.3-1. In addition to (a) medical provisions by the
CCs (medical screening examination and annual additional examinations for all construction workers
with treatment if needed), the control program includes (b) killing of rats in the construction areas by
YRCH (where the rats are attracted by the food supplies) to maintain rat densities within standards
levels.
Details for (a) (b) are discussed as follows:
(a) Medical Checking: This is carried out once a year on behalf of the CCs by YRCH (See
Table 4.2.6.3.1-1).
(b) Rat Killing in Construction Zone: Monitoring of rat density was conducted by Yellow
River Central Hospital and Henan Anti-epidemic Station in the construction area in 1993. The results
indicated that the density was as high as 30.8%, which was not only higher than the rat hazard-free
standard (3%) in rural China, but also higher than the background value of 16.7% without the project.
The hazard of outbreak of rat-borne diseases is high at such a high density. In this case, a health and
anti-epidemic team, jointly established by Yellow River Central Hospital and Henan Anti-epidemic
Station, carried out a rat killing catnpaign with rat poison in May 1995, which covered all camps at the
Site. The density was monitored and measured at 1.4% thereafter. Rat killing campaigns were again
conducted in November 1995 and May 1996, when the rat density was measured at 0.8% and 0.68%,
both meeting the rat hazard-free standard for rural China. History of rat density variations is as shown
in Figure 4.2.6.3.3-1. Rat killing in the construction zone will be undertaken again in April 1997 by
YRCH.
Inspection of Figure 4.2.6.3.3-1 indicates that the rat killing by YRCH does reduces the rat
density to levels below without project conditions, but it also indicates that not long after each rat killing
campaign the rat density moves upward to levels above the standard, and remains above until the next
rat killing, and indeed is above the standard at this time (Feb. 97). The indication is that the rat killing
must be conducted more frequently than now being practiced by YRCH.
4.2.6.4 Enteric and Other Diseases Control in Construction Zone
The control program here is to maintain sanitary conditions in the construction work and
living areas, including adequate/safe water supply and adequate management of wastes including
liquid wastes (sanitary and industrial) and solid waste (sanitary and industrial). In addition air
pollution must be duly controlled. All of these controls are discussed in Section 4.2.2 on
Environmental Management of Dam Construction.
4-20



4.2.6.5 Diseases Control in Reservoir Region Around Reservoir/ESA
4.2.6.5.1 Diseases Statistics for Region
In 1985 YRCH, in cooperation with related agencies, investigated communicable diseases in
the reservoir area and vicinity, including malaria, encephalitis, hemorrhagic fever, and enteric
diseases, including 53 townships in 8 counties of Shanxi and Henan provinces. Figure 4.2.6.5.1-1
shows investigated counties and townships in the two provinces. Ten years later, in 1995, this activity
was conducted again to see if changes had occurred. As illustrated in Table 4.2.6.5.1- 1, the incidence
of communicable diseases did not change very much and is lower now because of improved sanitary
conditions.
4.2.6.5.2 Malaria and Encephalitis
As noted earlier, if mosquito larval monitoring and disease statistics indicate any increases of
incidence of malaria and/or encephalitis in the region, then ERD/YRCH must investigate whether
this may be due to the hydrologic changes caused by XP and if so, to plan and implement the needed
control measures in cooperation with provincial health departments.
One of the key measures for controlling mosquito bone diseases is periodic monitoring of the
pertinent mosquito species larvae. If the larvae incidence for any of these species is increased, or if
larvae are found for other diseases vector mosquito not formerly found in the area, then monitoring
control measures must be undertaken. The EMO plan to start this larvae monitoring within the next
three months.
The program for control of the mosquito bone diseases in the ESA is shown in Figure
4.2.6.5.2-1.
4.2.6.5.3 Hemorrhagic Fever
The problem is that the XP, unless there are suitable controls, will increase the rat density
and thus the hazard of hemorrhagic fever, in the peripheral zone around the reservoir estimated at 5
kmn from the reservoir. The rat density can be expected to increase in this zone due to (i) the available
of food in the construction zone greatly increases rat density in the zone, and some of these rats will
find their way uphill, and (ii) reservoir inundation will force the rats uphill. It is necessary to protect
families living in the peripheral area, by monitoring the rat density to see whether there are any
significant increases, and if so, to undertake rat killing to reduce the rat density to without project
condition. This measure, as shown in Figure 4.2.6.5.3-1, will be started by EMO within the next three
months.
4.2.6.5.4 Enteric Diseases in RS Villa,ees
The control program here is to maintain sanitary conditions in the RS villages as discussed in
Section 4.2.3. As pointed out in Chapter 5, the Role of the RPDI team of ECIs should be expanded to
cover compliance with EIA stipulations for design, construction, and operation of water supply,
waste management, drainage, and other facilities in RS villages and/or host areas which house
resettler families. This is considered to be the only practicable way to achieve control of this
problem. The enteric diseases control program is described in Section 4.2.3.
4.2.7   Other SEIs
4.2.7.1 Fisheries
Because of its very high silt content, the Yellow River has never had a significant natural
fishery in its freshwater reaches downstream of the Loess Plateau. In the past there has been a valuable
fishery in the estuary and coastal areas which was dependent on fresh water and nutrients provided by
the river. Although there are little available data on this fishery, the available information suggests that
its importance has declined over the past several decades. The present situation is summarized below.
4.2.7.1.1 Riverine Fishery
(a) Capture Fishery
Although the Yellow River is a major watercourse it has never been of significance as a fishery
resource simply because the river is much too muddy to support any significant commercial populations
of fish. Additional problems include extremely low flows in some years, increasing levels of pollution,
and changes in flows and scouring subsequent to construction of Sanmenxia. As a result the numbers of
families depending on the riverine fishery livelihood in the reaches below Xiaolangdi dam has been
4-21



relatively small and the catch per unit effort appears to have declined steadily since the 1950s (Table
4.2.7.1-1). Even in the towns and cities along the river the large majority of the population are occupied
in full time agriculture and other landbased pursuits. Only 2% of the population engages in fishing and
this is on a part time basis. The productivity of the natural fishery is so small that official statistics are
not kept.
The Xiaolangdi project will result in some increases in low flows and in less turbid water
during the dry season of the year (Tables 4.2.7.1-2, 3 and 4), but is not likely to have a significant
adverse impact on the existing freshwater fishery. The impact, if any, should be positive.
(b) Aquaculture
Aquaculture, on the other hand, is relatively well developed on both banks of the river and is
much more important to the economy than the natural fishery. According to unofficial statistics there
are 441,000 mu of fish ponds along the lower reaches of the Yellow River and the annual yield is
approximately 6.6 million kg. This is some 5% of the agricultural output for the same area.
The Xiaolangdi Project has the potential of significantly improving opportunities for fish pond
aquaculture by ensuring a firm water supply during the dry season. There is particular potential for
expansion of this important activity in the areas near the river where land elevation is below that of the
river, where water could be easily supplied to the ponds by gravity. An alternative is for improved use
of groundwater. Evaluation of the potentials for such increased aquaculture production would require an
investment in the order of 150,00 yuan for the planning study at the feasibility level as noted in Section
5.3. It is now estimated that an investmnent of approximately 8,000 yuan per mu of pond would be
required for pond development, which could result in a meaningful benefit both in economic (a net
return of 1,500 yuan/mu/year) and social terms.
4.2.7.1.2 Estuarine Fishery
While in the past the estuary reportedly supported an important fishery, it has been greatly
depreciated over the past century because of decreasing river flows into the estuary, even zero flows
sometimes in the dry season, as irrigation and urban/industrial withdrawals and levels of pollution have
steadily increased. There can be little doubt this has resulted in serious impairment of the estuarine
fishery potential. The impact of XP is not expected to be significant. It will be positive to the extent that
river fresh water flow actually reaches the estuary. This may be small, however, because of steadily
increasing demands for abstraction/use of the river water for other purposes.
4.2.7.1.3 Coastal Fishery
For the same reasons noted above the fishery in the coastal waters off the mouth of the Yellow
River should not be significantly affected by the Xiaolangdi Project. Although there will be a
considerable reduction in flow volume to the sea during the month of October, the spawning period for
most important commercial species is between April and July when flows will not be less than under
natural conditions and may possibly be higher.
4.2.7.1.4 New Reservoir Fisherv
The Xiaolangdi reservoir will be operated in a manner which will permit settling out of silt and
formation of a relatively clear water body during the period from October to June. Possibilities for fish
production in the clear water zone are currently being investigated. Because the relatively cold water
temperature throughout much of the October through June period, however, it is unlikely that any
valuable commercial fishery will be possible. It may be possible, with special management methods, to
introduce/utilize species which would support sport fishing. If so this could contribute to the
development of the recreation/tourism potential in the area. This potential is to be explored in the
proposed follow-up study on the feasibility of establishing a recreational operation in and around the
new reservoir (See Section 5.3).
4.2.7.2 Liandi Cofferdam
The overall XP plan includes the Liandi cofferdam, which is located 5 km downsteam from the
Main Dam. This cofferdam was completed in March 1993 and resulted in changes in the flow in the
Liandi river (a branch of the Yellow River), particularly a decrease in flow volume north of the
cofferdam. This resulted in (i) reduction in the valuable riverine fishery at Jiyuan, (ii) impairment of
drinking water supply, and (iii) reduction in irrigation water supply causing a 20% decrease in summer
4 - 22



wheat yield in the Potou Xiang and Jili District south of the cofferdam. To compensate for these losses
YRWHDC utilized special measures to increase the water supply for the fishery and provided funds for
compensating for cropping losses.
4.2.7.3 Water Ouality
4.2.7.3.1 Downstream Water Quality Effects by Construction
River water monitoring results from the Mengjin Bridge, Huayuankou, Gaocun, and Lijin
Hydrometric Stations during 1992 to 1996 are shown in Table 4.2.7.3.3-1. These indicate that all the
parameters meet the Category III surface water quality standards as included in national standards
(Table 4.2.7.3.1-1), except that nitrite and BOD sometimes exceed the standards, and that cadmium
exceeds the standard during the wet season. These data indicate that the project construction has not
caused any adverse effects on river water quality. When the reservoir is created, the flow discharge will
be enlarged during the dry season, which will improve the water quality in the lower reaches.
4.2.7.3.2 Downstream Water Ouality Effects of Reservoir
The results of the water quality study show that the only significant changes in water quality
parameters due to impoundment will be (i) reduction in downstream silt content (turbidity) in the dry
season discharges, and (ii) decrease in downstream water temperature due to storage of water during the
winter period (Table 4.2.7.3.2-1). The first of these will be very beneficial in controlling river bed
siltation in the lower reaches. The predicted decrease in water temperature of approximately 4.1
degrees C is not expected to have any adverse effect on crops during the irrigation season. This is
because experience in the area in irrigation of crops with groundwater, which has temperatures about
40C lower than the surface water, has not shown any adverse effect on crop yield.
A special study will be completed to determnine the extent and scope of reservoir clearing which
might be required to prevent contamination of reservoir waters subsequent to the flooding of
contaminated areas such as sanitary, hospital, and industrial waste disposal sites.
4.2.7.3.3 Water Ouality Monitoring in Lower Reaches
Taking account of the study findings on water quality, the necessary monitoring program
(Figure 4.2.7.3.3-1) includes periodic monitoring for temperature changes (once per month) and
periodic complete mineral analysis of the downstream river water at several existing downstream
stations. Monitoring of these is done three times per year, once during each of the periods of minimum,
average, and maximum river flow. Analysis should include Total Dissolved Solids (TDS), all major
cations (Ca, Mg, Na, K) and all major anions (HCO3, S04, Cl, NO3) as well as pH, temperature, and
silt content/turbidity, plus any other parameters required by MWR. In addition once yearly analysis will
be made of sediments of the reservoir, river bed, and estuary to monitor accumulations of non-
degradable pesticides and of any toxic heavy metals being mined in the upper river basin. Table
4.2.7.3.3-1 summarizes water quality monitoring by YRWRPB for 1992-1996. This is still the pre-
EIA/92 monitoring without adjustment to meet EIA/92 requirements. This modification should be made
immediately.
4.2.7.4 Land Accretion in Coastal Area
Due to the deposition of sediments at the mouth of the river the delta increases in extent by
some 25 to 30 square kilometers each year. With the construction of the dam and the trapping of
sediments this process will be slowed to some extent. The Expert Panel for XP Design has evaluated
this impact as follows: "The rate of land creation at the river mouth area depends mainly on the
sediment volumes passing through Lijin. Before construction of the Xiaolangdi Project, the annual
sediment load would be about 0.8 x 109 tons at Lijin in the year 2000. After completion of the
Xiaolangdi Project, the reservoir will retain 10 x 109 tons of sediment and get a benefit of deposition
reduction of 7.8 x 109 tons at lower reaches in 50 years, that will result in a reduction of sediment load
of 2.2 x 109 tons coming to the estuarine area. But in the period of sediment detention of Xiaolangdi
Reservoir, the sediment concentration of diverted flow along the lower reaches will be less than that in
the natural condition. So, reducing sediment load at river mouth in 50 years only amounts 2 x 109 tons,
about 5% of total sediment load running to the estuary area without Xiaolangdi project. The influence
on the sedimentation at river mouth due to Xiaolangdi Project is quite small."
4.2.7.5 Resources to be Inundated
4-23



The area to be inundated covers approximately 278 km2 and will affect downstream land
uses approximately as follows: About 185,100 mu of farmland (4.1 percent of the total cultivated
land), 9.7 percent of the forest land. 12 percent of the forage land, 13 percent of the barren lands, and
10.6 percent of the ponds. The towns, industries, and infrastructure to be displaced include 173
villages, numerous small industrial and mining enterprises. 12 small power station, 267 irrigation
pumping stations, 658 km of irrigation canals, 688 km of highways, 548 km of communication lines,
and 4.59 million m2 of housing.
Reservoir formation will inundate sizable deposits of mineral resources including coal,
bauxite, iron, sulfur, gypsum, phosphorus, and cobalt. The locations of these are shown in Figure
3.2.10-1 of Chapter 3. The only potentially significant loss would be coal since some 332 million
tons will be submerged under the reservoir. However, because the coal lies beneath impermeable
shale strata, some mining of it will remain feasible after inundation.
4.2.7.6 Landslides in Reservoir
Bank slumps and landslides are not numerous in the reservoir areas. The total volume of
earth subject to sliding or caving-in the reservoir area is relatively small, about 105 million tons over
an area of about 5.6 km2. The principal areas of concem are between Hedi and Lingshang villages
near the Banjian River, as well as on the south bank of the river approximately 4 km upstream of the
dam. Although bank slumps and slides are expected to occur from time to time in these areas, no
significant impacts are foreseen because the danger areas have been well identified, and there will be
no villages close enough to be affected.
The freeboard height of the dam has been designed to resist the range of wave heights that
would be generated by the maximum predicted landslide into the reservoir. The design was based on
surge tests performed to simulate slump/slide conditions. The results indicate that (a) the maximum
height of surge due to landslides at 180 m cofferdam water level is 4 m, the cofferdam crest level is
set at 185 m in the present design, thus meeting the safety requirements; (b) when the reservoir water
level is at 275 m, the maximum height of surge is I m, the dam crest level is set at 281 m, which,
plus a 1.2 m breast wall, will prevent dam flooding by surge; (c) the maximum height of surge will
be 4 m at 275 m along the full length of the reservoir, considering that the dangerous areas have been
identified and that there are no settlements in the affected areas, no significant effects should result
from any bank slumps or landslides.
4.2.7.7 Reservoir Seepage
The project design includes a rigid concrete cutoff wall and single-row grouting curtains
which are intended to cut off possible reservoir seepage. The main dam cutoff wall is 80 m upstream
of the dam axis. This wall is 438.6 m long and 1.2 m wide, covering a total surface area of 21,800
m2. The maximum depth is 82 m. The wall body penetrates into the core by 12 m, with 20 m at the
ends connected with the core. Reinforcing cages are arranged in the upper 18 m partion of the wall.
The curtain line is placed 80 m upstream of the dam axis, and connected with the cutoff wall in the
river course. Because the foundation level of the declined core tends to increase toward the river
banks and transits to the vertical core, the curtain line approaches and finally goes in parallel with the
dam axis before it extends 4 m further upstream.
The left rock mass is thin and weak, where the water release and power generation structures
are located. This is the critical location for seepage around the dam. To achieve effective seepage
control, grouting holes are used which penetrate sufficiently deep into the bedrock to prevent any
significant seepage. Also, water release and power generation structures are concentrated in the area
between the left abutment and the saddle dam. To control the seepage water pressure in the vicinity
of tunnels downstream of the curtain line, the curtain is designed to be as deep as 150 m.
The massive right rock mass is not susceptible to seepage around the dam. However, there
are many faults on the right bank, and there are zones of high permeability of considerable depth on
both sides of the faults. All these require special curtain treatment. To plug the fissures in the rock
surface of the core foundation, to achieve better curtain grouting performance, and to avoid seepage
failure in line with the surface of the core foundation, consolidation grouting holes are arranged in
the full bottom width of the core. These holes are vertical to the bed rock surface.
4 - 24



The above mentioned treatment measures will ensure that seepage from the reservoir will not
be a problem.
4.2.7.8 Reservoir Area Clearing
4.2.7.8.1 Scope of Work
For ensuring safe operation of the reservoir, controlling water quality pollution, protecting
public health of the downstream population, and making full use of the multipurpose benefits of the
project, necessary reservoir clearing will be carried out prior to reservoir filling. According to the
project construction schedule, reservoir clearing activities will be completed in three phases, as follows:
Phase I involves the areas to be inundated below El 180, covering a total of 79.29 km2, including 35.06
km2 in Jiyuan, 10.57 km2 in Mengjin, and 33.66 km2 in Xinan. Phases II and III cover the areas to be
inundated at El 180-275 m.
4.2.7.8.2 Clearing for Usual Dam Proiect
The usual clearing program for reservoirs in China, following government regulations, includes
the following components:
(a) Removal of Structures: All houses/dwelling caves and living amenities in the reservoir area
will be removed down to ground level. Any unsalvageable but floatable materials will be incinerated in
situ. Any power supply, telecommunications and public address line poles will be removed. Dykes and
dams obstacling navigation will be destroyed by blasting or removal, with the any remainder not being
higher than 0.5 m above the ground.
(b) Sanitary Clearing: Any wastes in toilets, septic tanks, livestock houses, rubbish heaps and
methane pits will be removed out of the reservoir area, or otherwise deeply buried after solarization for
50 days in the event of failure due to one reason or another. Any tombs made in the past 15 years will be
relocated out of the reservoir area or bumed in situ. Each will be disinfected using 0.5-1 km quicklime.
Those made more than 15 years ago will be handled as agreed with the owner. Such places as hospitals
and veterinary stations, which cause toxicity or serious pollution, will be managed as required by
health/epidemic and environmental protection authorities.
(c) Clearing of Forest: This part of clearing mainly involves continuous forestlands or sparse
trees. Trees will all be cut and removed out of the reservoir area. Any remaining stumps will not be 0.3
m higher than above the ground. Any floatable materials such as unusable straw and branch will be
incinerated in situ prior to the reservoir filling.
The estimated costs for clearing for Phases I, II, III, assuming the usual program as described
above, are shown in Table 4.2.7.8.2-1.
4.2.7.8.3 Clearing for XP
The clearing problem for XP is quite different from usual because of the fact that the Yellow
River is heavily silt laden (highest in world), hence much if not most of the reservoir area will quickly
be covered with a thick layer of mud which should completely protect the water above from
contamination from ground sources. Accordingly the XP Expert Panel on Environment and
Resettlement (Ref. 201.5) recommended that a special study be carried out to determine the clearing
program which will be appropriate for XP because in the judgment of the EEMEP, it is very likely that
such study will show that the clearing program costs can be greatly reduced. The TOR for the proposed
special study are given in Section 6.3.
4.2.7.9 Rare S2ecies and Special Habitats
4.2.7.9.1 Introduction
Figure 3.2.9-1 of Chapter 3 shows the existing situation in the reservoir region on precious eco-
resources in the reservoir region.
4.2.7.9.2 Rare Species and Special Habitas
There are a number of rare and protected species in the reservoir and surrounding area. These
include the giant swan, cygnet, mandarin duck, giant salamander, otter, and musk deer as well as golden
vulture and other birds of prey.
Waterfowl will be the only species of birds affected directly by the reservoir and depending
upon reservoir regulation. Suitable habitats should become established around the reservoir similar to
those presently existing along the river banks. Some increase in waterfowl populations may be possible
4-25



provided pressure from hunting or habitat destruction does not increase. Giant salamander, otter, and
musk deer are found in relatively remote habitats in the headwaters of small streams and forested
rugged terrain outside of the area to be flooded, and will not be affected directly by the project.
Resettlement is planned in urban areas and areas which are already dedicated to agriculture.
Intensification of development in such areas should not affect areas critical to these species. The
reservoir will not flood any nesting sites of raptors as these are mostly in high cliffs above the reservoir.
4.2.7.9.3 Eco-SwanRs
(a) Existing Situation
All valuable wetlands and wildlife reserves are beyond the area affected by the reservoir. A
number of them however are found along the downstrem reaches and in the delta of the Yellow River
(Figure 3.2.9-1 of Chapter 3). The marshlands in the old course of the Yellow river and those near
Liuyuankou are internationally recognized and important for large numbers of migrating waterbirds. As
these are not contiguous with the river and do not depend upon it for their water supply or regulation,
they should not be adversely affected.
The downstream areas and estuary delta were included in the first group of national protection
wetlands as announced in 1995. The total surface area is 153,000 ha, including 113,720 ha land (74.3%)
and 39,280 ha water (25.7%, including shallow sea and fresh waters). In this natural reserve there live
such protected animals as grus japonensis, gray crane, white stork, giant swan, golden eagle, giant
bustard, accipitridae, tattle, wild goose, gull, and mandarin duck.. The protected plants include wild
bean, natural robinia pseudoacacia forests, natural willow forests, natural reed marshes, and seepweed.
Distribution of these plants is as shown in Figure 3.2.9-1 of Chapter 3.
As noted in Chapter 3, zero flow events occurred in the lower reaches during 18 of the 24-year
period from 1972 to 1995, with a total duration of 499 days or 27.7 days per year. The maximum
duration lasted 122 days. Such events are seasonal, generally occurring during the February to May dry
season. In the case of such an event, there is no vertical groundwater recharge, resulting in local water
table reduction. However, some river runoff and rain are retained in the river course, and in the low-
lying ponds and depressions on both banks. Hence zero river flow causes little effect on the wetlands in
the delta. Plants in this reserve are predominantly salt-resistant species. The mineral concentration of the
groundwater will be increased in the short run, but the increase will not be adequate to pose significant
effects on the plants here. No adverse impacts will be subsequently caused on the habitats and migration
zones of birds.
The reservoir will increase the water discharge during dry seasons, mitigate the zero flow
instances in the lower reaches, and result in improved water availability in the estuarine delta. Thus the
XP will be helpful to some extent for the existence and development of the wetlands here.
The existing wetland reserves in Wuli, Weifang, and Shouguang counties in the delta will not
be threatened as the dam will release more water in periods when flows are typically low. The impact on
water levels, water availability, and coastal habitats is not likely to be significant, and any such impacts
will be positive.
(b) Proposed SRecial Study
A number of special studies are proposal for evaluating attractive potentials whereby the XP
can effectively contribute to enhancement of environmental values in the region. One of these is a
proposed study, discussed in Section 5.3, to evaluate the potentials for including in XP a component
for improving the wetlands in the lower basin region.
4.2.7.9.4 Forest Eco-Values
The reservoir area and the construction zone are both located in hillyland where there is a
long history of cultivation. Original forests have been all destroyed, without little meaningful forest
remaining. The affected lower reaches belong to the North China Plain most of which is cultivated
land. Vegetation is secondary shrubs even in the limited low-mountain and hillyland areas, without
any significant natural forests. However, as noted in Section 6.3, a special study is recommended for
evaluating the feasibility of reestablishing some forest over the reservoir as part of a purposed
recreational park.
4.2.7.10 Global Environmental Issues
4 -26



4.2.7.10.1 Salient Issues
The salient issues are earthwarming ozone depletion, and biodiversity. These are discussed as
follows:
(a) Earthwarminz
The XP will have a significant effect on the global warming situation in that the hydropower
generated will significantly reduce the national need for generating electricity from coal. This is
especially important in view of China's position as the leading coal-burning country in the world.
The generation of hydroelectricity by Xiaolangdi will be equivalent to the use of 1.9 million tons of
coal per annum, which would otherwise be burned. This is equivalent to saving emission of 4.6
million tons of carbon dioxide which contributes to global warming, of 33,000 tons of sulfur dioxide
which contribute to acid precipitation on a regional scale, and of 18,700 tons nitrogen oxide which
contribute to photochemical smog in the region. It will eliminate the management of 534,000 tons of
waste residues per year. China presently produces 1,900 million tons of carbon dioxide per annum
(10 percent of the global emissions). The XP hydropower generation will amount to about 0.4 percent
of total national electricity needs, and will reduce use of coal for generating power on a national basis
by about 0.6 percent.
(b) Ozone Deletion
The XP should not result in any significant effect on the global ozone depletion problem.
(c) Biodiversity
The XP will not result in impairment of any precious ecological resources in the EIA, hence
will not result in any adverse effect on biodiversity. All of the ESA is a "humanized" area with little
remaining precious ecology except for some wetlands in the lower portion of the lower basin. The XP
is not expected to result in any changes in these (See Section 4.2.9.3) except that a special study is
proposed (Section 6.3) for evaluating the wetlands situation and for determining whether XP should
include an extra component for enhancing the wetlands.
4.2.7.10.2 International Environmental Azreements
Annex 4.2.7.10-1 is a listing of some 20 international environmental agreements in which
China is participating. Review of these with respect to effects of XP indicates that the XP will not
result in any effects which are inconsistent with these agreements. In particular the XP will contribute
to lessening earthwarming inputs from China by reducing China's use of coal for power production.
4.2.8  Proiect Benefits
4.2.8.1 Flood Control
4.2.8.1.1 Changes in River Hvdrologv
Changes in the River hydrology resulting from the Project are shown in Figure 4.2.8.1.1-1.
This shows the monthly variation in level of Xiaolangdi Reservoir, and Figure 4.2.8.1.1-2 shows the
monthly discharge from the Dam at Xiaolangdi for average and design conditions. The significance
of the XP is that it will, for the first time in history, enable effective control (in combined operation
with Sanmenxia and other control works already built) of the Yellow River in its lower reaches and
thus make possible the great benefits which are discussed below and summarized in Section 4.2.9.1.
4.2.8.1.2 Effectiveness of Flood Control
The XP, above all else, has the objective of achieving effective control of frequent serious
and dangerous flooding of the entire lower basin occupied by some 80 million people and associated
agriculture and industry. This critical need has existed for centuries, and has progressively become
more and more critical as population, urbanization, agriculture, and industry have all markedly
increased in recent decades and as the lower basin's levee protection system has been made
increasingly vulnerable by river siltation. The Samnenxia Project was intended to fill this gap, but the
record shows that Sanmenxia is far from enough and that XP is critically needed.
The combined flood regulating operation of Xiaolangdi Reservoir with Sanmenxia, Luhun,
and Guxian reservoirs will lower the 1: 1,000 year flood peak to 22.500 m3/s at Huayuankou, and
enable control without using the Beijindi Detention Basin (See Figure 4.2.8.1.2-1). Floods of 22.000
m3/s at Huayuankou can be reduced to less than 10.000 m3/s, which greatly increases flood control
safety along the lower reaches and mitigates losses on flooded land. The Xiaolangdi Reservoir can
4 -27



also effectively cut down the flood peak of one in 10,000 years to less than 27.350 m31s at
Huayuangkou, which mitigates the menace of flood in the lower reaches and reduces the probability
of flood storage operation of Sanmenxia Reservoir from once in 10 years to once in 100 years.
In summary, the XP will change the overall existing system of flood protection in the lower
basin from a once/60 year flood to a once/1,000 year flood.
4.2.8.1.3 Flood Forecasting and Emergency Response (See Figures 4.2.8.1.3-1 and 2)
A properly planned flood control system includes not only construction and operation of
physical flood control works, but also a system for Flood Forecasting and an Emergency Response on
what to do in the event of an actual flooding emergency. These are discussed below.
(a) Flood Forecasting
The YRCC Flood Forecasting System (FFS) is believed to be among the most advanced for
major rivers in the world. Such forecasting is especially important for the Yellow River because the
rapid confluence time of flows which create floods in the lower basin. The Hydrological Bureau of
YRCC is responsible for managing the hydrometric station network in the Yellow River basin,
including hydrometry, flood condition reporting, and flood forecasting. The overall basin is divided
into five survey area offices operated by the Hydrological Bureau, which, supported by prefectural
and provincial agencies, furnish the salient information for computer processing by the Hygrological
Bureau. At the present time these communications are carried out by use of (i) the national
telecommunications system, (ii) short-wave radio transmitters and receivers at the key hydrological
stations, and (iii) a three-level radio network for the survey area along the middle reaches, for
supporting the above-mentioned communications.
The flood forecasting procedure comprises two components, namely (i) hydrological
forecasting and (ii) meteorological forecasting (including long-, medium-, and short-term forecasting.
The medium-term forecasting predicts rainfall for the coming 10 days, and the short-term forecasting
for the coming 3 days. The flood forecasting, based on the meteorological forecasting, is focused on
the lower basin region below Sanmenxia and also on floods in the north section of the main stem and
the tributary Weihe River. The predictions are for peak flows, time of their occurrence, flood
hydrograph, volume, and water level. The forecasting procedures for the lower basin include warning
forecasting, rainfall runoff forecasting, and flood forecasting. All of these make optimal use of
computer technology.
(b) Flood Emereencv Response System.
Figure 4.2.8.1.3-1 shows the organization of YRCC's Flood Emergency Response System
(F/ERS), which has been developed over the past 40 years. This is the result of much experience over
the past several hundred years in protecting against flows in the lower reaches of the Yellow River.
This has focused on the salient problem of organizing the population at risk into "shock brigades" to
combat flood emergencies. The saying along the channel of the Yellow River is that "dikes are
valueless without people to take care of them" The achievement made in past decades in combating
many flood emergencies not only owes much to the engineering contribution in dikes and control
gates, but equally to the people-based forces for flood protection, which have developed the
capability for organizing swifty and knowing what to do. YRCC records indicated that a complete
setup has been formed for organizations at all levels to carry out the emergency work, including a
system of command posts and specialized units needed for coordinating the inputs of all combat
mechanisms.
Figure 4.2.8.1.3-2 furnishes additional details, including information on flood forecasting and
public warning activities related to emergency flood combat operations. At present, as part of the
planning for the Xiaolangdi Project, YRCC is working on further strengthening the F/ERS by
reorienting the entire system to be computer-based. This computerization is of great importance
because of the need for obtaining prompt decision from the higher authority, including the State
Council, on making the kinds of difficult and painful decisions that must be urgently made at critical
moments for optional flood combat operation, including "which areas to let flood in order to protect
other areas with higher priority protection need." This instant availability of a unified coordinated
command center is of most importance for ensuring that all agencies and all people will be willing to
4 28



work together effectively, taking into account their divergence of interests that have become
increasingly apparent over the past several decades due to profound social changes, including
intensive urbanization and industrialization.
4.2.8.2 Ice-Run Control
Ice jams in the river frequently cause flooding and result in loss of life and property damage.
The situation is managed now mainly by releasing water from Sanmenxia Reservoir to break the
jams, but the capacity of Sanmenxia Reservoir is only 1.8 billion m3, limiting the ability to control
the ice run hazard.
The combined operation of Xiaolangdi and Sannenxia Reservoirs will relieve, if not
eliminate, the ice-jam and flood menace along the lower reaches. The combined operation will make
it unnecessary to store water for ice run control in Sanmenxia Reservoir in 70 percent of the years
because this role can be undertaken by Xiaolangdi Reservoir. This will reduce siltation in Saumnenxia
Reservoir and increase winter energy output of the Sarmenxia hydroelectric plant,
4.2.8.3 Silt Regulation
The lower reaches of Yellow River have been continuing to accumulate silt, requiring higher
and higher dikes with increased hazards of breaching each year. The effect of Xiaolangdi will be to
stabilized the downstream riverbed for a period of at least 20 years. This would defer the cost of
raising the dikes, and allow other solutions to be developed. The economic benefit (at 1991 prices)
has been estimated at Y 1.06 billion.   With respect to downstream siltation, it should be noted that
XP will control this problem for a period of some 20 to 25 years following completion of XP
construction. However, XP is only the first of three additional major dams to be built on the main
Yellow River stem, with the second at Quikou, upstream of XLD, and the third in-between at
Longmen, and the three together will control siltation for about 100 years from 2001. In this 100-year
period it is expected that the upstream soil erosion in the Loess Plateau will been put under control so
that the overall situation will become stabilized.
4.2.8.4 Water SuRDlv
(a) Irrigation
The irrigation areas to be supplied by the Xiaolangdi Reservoir are both around the reservoir
itself and downstream along the course of the River. The areas around the reservoir totaling
approximately 330,000 mu, will help solve the resettlement problem. The irrigated areas downstream
will total 33 million mu, representing a large increase over the 5-10 million mu that can be irrigated
with the water presently available. Xiaolangdi will remarkable improve irrigation water supply and
economic conditions for thousands of farm families.
(b) Municipal and Industrial Demand
The municipal and industrial demand for water in the lower reaches is expected to reach
about 3 billion m3 by the year 2000. This can be fully satisfied by the XP.
4.2.8.5 Surface and Groundwater Balance
At present, industry and agriculture in some of the downstream areas depend on
groundwater. Excessive groundwater pumping is resulting in serious reduction of groundwater levels.
Without Xiaolangdi, the situation can be expected to get worse as the river flow continues to decline
with increasing demand in the irrigation season. The XP presents the opportunity to reduce the
pressure on the groundwater resources. A special study is proposed to evaluate this problem in order
to prepare a program for overall water utilization which will represent an optimal balance in use of
these two water supply sources. This is described in Section 6.3.
4.2.8.6 Electricity Generation
The total installed capacity of the Xiaolangdi hydroelectric plant is 1,800 MW, with an
annual average energy output of 5.4 billion kWh. The plant location is central among the cities that
would be served, including Zhengzhou, Luoyang, Xinxiang, and Sanmenxia. This will be a new
power source for Henan Province and will enhance economic and socioeconomic development in the
region.
4 - 29



Hydropower is a renewable energy resource and the plant will not pollute the environment.
An equivalent thermal plant would use 1.9 million tons of coal annually and release 33,000 tons of
sulfur dioxide, 18,700 tons of nitrogen oxide, and 534,000 tons of residue.
4.2.9  Other Evaluations
4.2.9.1 Environmental Economics
The purposes of the Environmental Economic Analysis are three, (i) to ensure that all
environmental losses to result from the project are included in the benefit/cost analysis, (ii) to review
the distribution of benefits between different groups of people as related to relative affluence (will
the project make "the rich richer and the poor poorer"?), and (iii) to assess the impact on the project's
overall benefit and cost ratios, which result from including EPMs in the overall project as compared
to not including the EPMs, in order to show that including the EPMs does not impair the project
benefit and cost relationship.
(a) Inclusion of Environmental Losses in Economic Analysis: Review of the XP Economic
Analysis by the EIA Team indicates that all known environmental losses (and/or gains) have been
included in the analysis, including costs for resettlement, value of inundated properties and mineral
resources, etc.
(b) Proiect Beneficiaries: As emphasized, XP is, first and primarily, a flood protection
project with the objective, for the first time, in history, of protecting the lower basin people and
related agriculture, settlements, and industry from frequent devastating flooding. This protection
applies equally to all people in the lower basin.
The second main benefit of XP is irrigation. This is focused on improving the livelihoods of
the rural population. The third major benefit is hydropower, and while this will go mainly for urban
and industrial uses, these uses are widely distributed among all population affluence levels in the
sense that he rural poor who move to cities need jobs in industry and need electrification of their
homes.
The total XP benefits will therefore be well shared by the nonaffluent population in the lower
basin.
(c) Imoact of EPMs on Proiect Benefit and Cost: The EIA/92 report included a comparison
of project costs versus benefits for two conditions, with and without inclusion of costs of the
specified EPMs (meaning environmental protection measures beyond what is included in
conventional/pre-EIA project planning). This indicated that the overall benefit/cost ratio with EPMs
included was approximately 1.44, and without the EPMs approximately 1.33, representing and
improvement of about 8 percent gain in the benefit/cost ratio. The RPDI is now updating the project's
economic analysis including reestimation of the information presented above.
4.2.9.2 Environmental Laws
Table 4.2.9.2-1 has been prepared to summarize the environmental laws and regulations of
PRC that apply to XP, and to indicate how these have been duly considered and complied with in
planning and implementation of XP. In addition, Table 4.2.9.2-2 shows the applicable laws and
regulations for the XLD/RS program and how these have been complied with.
4.2.9.3 Public Participation
4.2.9.3.1 Introduction
Public participation has become an important part of the EIA. The purpose of public
participation is to allow due and objective consideration of public opinions on the project planning and
implementation, and to improve environmental decision making by making full use the public
judgment. Public participation is a two-way information transmission system which should be
incorporated into the project planning from the very beginning. This provides the best way for
managing problems with which the public is seriously concerned.
Of the various environmental issues included in the XP/EIA, the question of Public
Participation is most important for the issue of Resettlement (RS). For this reason, the RS Team, in
developing the RS plan, has given particular attention to considering the opinions/wishes of the affected
resettler families and of the affected host communities. In most developing countries (DCs) the RS
planning is carried out by the Project Proponent together with the responsible governmental agencies
4-30



with the assumption that government officials give adequate consideration to the real needs of the
affected villagers. The experience of the DCs in general has shown that this is not enough, and that the
RS planning process must include some direct consultation with the affected villagers.
4.2.9.3.2 Overall Public Participation Program
Public participation of the affected people has been carried out included mainly by means of
media propagation, public lectures, consultation with government officials including grass roots
officials and with local residents, distribution of questionnaires, and direct interview with villagers. The
detailed tabulation on all this work is included in the present report as Supplemental Annex 4.2.9.3-1.
This is part of Volume II which comprises all of the supplemental annexes prepared for ElA/97.
The information given in Supplemental Annex 4.2.9.3-1 is summarized here in Figure 4.2.9.3- 1.
This shows that the overall Public Participation program, carried out over the period 1986 to 1997, has
been intensive, with a total of some 480 activities comprising meetings, reporting, consultations, field
visits, field surveys, and interviews with local villages.
4.2.9.3.3 Public Participation in RS
The public is of course generally concemed about all significant many issues involved in
planning/implementing major projects. For major dam/reservoir projects, as noted above, the major
concern is usually that of Resettlement. For this reason, the RS Team, in developing the RS plan, has
given particular attention to considering the opinions/wishes of the affected resettler families and of the
affected host communities. For these reasons the RS Team's work plan included direct consultation with
the affected villagers, as summarized in Figure 4.2.9.3-2. This included asking resettlers from household
to household for their opinions on resettlement destinations and resettlement programs. This part of
pubic participation involved some 173 village committees, 30 xiangs (townships) in the flooded 8
counties, including such activities as 152 public lectures, 500 interviews with villagers, and 50,000
questionnaires (80% return rate). These activities have allowed resettlers to understand the overall
project and its resettlement components, and also to express their opinions, recommendations, and
requirements. The results indicate that most resettlers are willing to move and are satisfied with the
resettlement planning. These recommendations and requirements served as a valuable guide in
formulating the detailed RS plan.
The work plan also included numerous discussions with government officials at the village
level as well as with officials at the higher township, county, prefecture, provincial, and national levels.
Thus the RS plan as developed is considered to be a "mix" of inputs from all levels, bottom to top and
top to bottom. including adequate consideration of resettler family wishes. In preparing the RS plan, the
guiding principle has been to ensure the resettlers that their new housing facilities and work place
facilities are planned to give a standard of living at least equal to their without-project conditions and in
addition to enable them to share in the overall project benefits. To ensure that the levels of
improvements/compensation to be carried out in project implementation are fair and adequate, the RS
plan includes formulation and execution of written agreements with the local officials concemed,
including the village level. Also continuing monitoring is carried out to check on this, including periodic
surveys and interviews directly with the affected population. In addition resettlers hove access to the
local RS offices for inquiring into any subjects of interest, including problems of grievances and
complaints. Also, resettlers can go to the court pursuant to the "Administrative Law of Suit" when they
think the decisions made by resettlement offices or govemment at various levels are unacceptable.
More detailed infornation on Public Participation in RS planning and implementation is given
in Section 4.2.3 ("Environmental Review of Resettlement").
4.2.9.3.4 Public Particivation by EIA Team/EMO
The work of the EIA team which prepared EIA/1992 included a large number of
consultations with government officials and individuals throughout the ESA, covering all of the
ElA/SEIs including natural resources, agriculture, urban settlements, industries, water supply, waste
management and pollution, etc., as well as resettlement. Thus the EIA team members were able
themselves to contribute significantly to the overall public participation process. This effort has been
continued by the EMO, established in 1994 and moreover the Expert Panel has reviewed the issue of
4 -31



public participation with the EMO and other XP officials concerned at the Panel's meetings held
every six months since October 1994.
4.2.9.4 Risk Management
The very detailed planning for XP covering a period of several decades, included preparation
of a comprehensive EIA in 1992 which delineated the various SEIs involved with XP and the various
specific EPMs which must be incorporated into the subsequent steps of detailed project design,
project construction. and project operations. Accordingly it is concluded that the overall project has
given due attention for the Risk Parameter in project planning and implementation to date. This is
shown in Figure 7.1-1, which presents a summary of EIA/97 with respect to all SEIs, and these SEIs
do also cover all significant risk issues. Figure 7.1-1 shows that due diligence has been given to
evaluation of and protecting against all significant risk issues, so that, assuming all of the EPMs
given in the EIA are duly carried out, the XP is in good shape in terms of risk management.
The assessment made in the present report, on how well the EIA/92/EPMs have been carried
out by XECC, shows, because of the pioneering nature of XP in term of incorporating such EPMs
into major dam project planning and implementation, that the XECC's environmental performance
has been getting underway and established during the Loan I period, and that considerably move
attention must be given to certain EPMs including those applicable for controlling dam construction
operations and for ensuring a sound RS program. Section 7.2 gives recommendations on
improvements which should be made as part of the Loan 2 Agreement. Assuming this is done, it is
believed fair to say that XP Risk Management will be quite adequate.
4.2.9.5 Environmental MIS
4.2.9.5.1 Review of Overall MIS in Oct. 96
The overall MIS is under development by RO. A recent evaluation of this by the Sociologist
Member of the Expert panel (Dr. K. Connor) (Ref. 203.5) prepared the following summary:
(a) The system (software and hardware) itself is good, but is probably overly complex for the
tasks it performs, largely data storage (mostly a one way flow into the system), display, and budget
management. These are certainly valuable applications, but the system itself is able support more
complex decision making and monitoring activities. The main weaknesses are (i) potential users are
unaware of ways the system (MS and GIS) can facilitate their work, (ii) no progress for sharing
information within and between agencies has been developed, and (iii) system development at
prefecture, province, and county RO offices seems overly ambitious given time, staff and financial
constraints.
(b) Monitorini Systems: A large body of monitoring data is being collected, but little
analysis is done. Competent analysis is critical for the system to serve its early warning function.
Both the monitoring processes, as well as ways the MIS can expedite the work of the responsible
agencies, need much more attention. In addition, it is unclear how the Project plans to use monitoring
data, for example, what will the Project actually do if incomes decline or complaints go unresolved?
(c) Environmental Parameters
The MIS can assist the EMO be a sub-system to manage environmental monitoring field
data. The system would handle the categories of data such as following:
Location                                  Data
Resettlement sites    Water supply, solid waste management, toilets and excreta
management, drainage channels, roads, power supply, clinics,
schools, ect.
Construction sites    Living areas (including water supply, sewage management, solid
waste, drainage); dust control on roads; production areas wastewater
management, noise control, occupation related health and safety
issues, ect.
Details on the environmental information to be included in the sub-system are given in the
Oct. 1996 report of the EEMEP (Ref. 201.5)
4.2.9.5.2 Present Assessment
4-32



One of the most important tasks in the present report or EIA/97 is the Environmental Review
of Resettlement as discussed in Section 4.2.3. To prepare this review if was necessary to obtain
updated information on the RS program, but much of the information now available from RO is not
yet updated despite the fact that RO has an RSJMIS system in use. The recommendation here is that
the RS/MIS group should be given technical assistance is how to use their MIS equipment effectively
for its intended purpose, namely to have reliable updating information available at all times.

FIGURE 4.2.7.3.3-1: YELLOW RIVER WATER OUALITY MONITORING PROGRAM
Item                                     Tasks and Requirements
1. Station Network                      Existing stations downstream will be used for monitoring water
quality downstream and in the estuary.
(See Figure 4.2.7.3-2)
2. Monitoring Items                     According to the Criteria for Water Quality Monitoring issued
by MWR, the items to be monitored include: water temperature,
(a) Monitoring of Water Quality     suspended load, pH value, conductivity, oxidation reduction
potential, CO2, DO, BOD, COD, ammonia nitrogen. nitrite
nitrogen, total contents of iron, phosphorus, and alkali, total
hardness, Cl, and SO4, total contents of Ca, Mg, K, Na, amounts
of volatile phenol, cyanide, arsenic, six-valence chromium,
cadmium, copper, lead, and mercury, coliform bacteria, and total
bacteria.
(b) Monitoring of Bottom Sediments
Heavy metals and other toxics.
3. Frequency of Monitoring              For 2 (a): 3 times yearly (minimum, average, maximum flows).
For 2 (b): Annually.
4. Reporting System                     To make monthly reports to EMO, and expeditiously report
unusual events.
5. Implementor                          The Xiaolangdi Station under YRCC Hydrographic Bureau.
6. Supervisor                           EMO under the YRWHDCRO.

TABLE 4.2.7.3.1-1: NEPA STANDARDS OF WATER QUALITY FOR DOMESTIC USE (1990)
No.                 Item                                      Standard
Sensory Index:
I    Color                       under 15 degrees, and peculiar colors shall be excluded
2     Muddiness                   below 5 degrees
3    Fume & Smell                free of any peculiar fume or smell
4     Visible Suspended Material   free of such substances
Chemical Index:
5    pH                          6.5-8.5
6    Total Hardness               below 250 mg/L
7    Iron                        below 0.3 mg/L
8    Manganese                   below 0.1 mg/L
9    Copper                      below 1.0 mg/L
I 0    Zinc                       below 1.0 mg/L
I I    Volatile Phenol            below 0.002 mg/L
12    Synthetic Anion Detergent    below 0.3 mg/L
Toxic Index
13    Fluoride                    below 1.0 mg/L, 0.5-1.0 mg/L is allowable
14    Cyanide                     below 0.05 mg/L
15    Arsenic                     below 0.04 mg/L
16    Selenium                    below 0.01 mg/L
17    Mercury                     below 0.001 mg/L
18    Cadmium                     below 0.0 lmg/L
19    Chromium (6-valence)        below 0.05mg/L
20    Lead                        below 0. Img/L
Bacteriological Index
21    Total Amount of Bacteria    less than 100/L(water)
22    Colon Bacillus Swarm        less than 3/L
23    Free Residual Chorine       no less than 0.3 mg/L when exposed for 30 minutes; if central water supply
system is used, it shall be no less than 0.05 mg/L at outlets while 0.3 mg/L
has to be met when water is released out of waterworks.
Note:   Quality of water supplied decentrally shall meet the toxic indexes provided for in the list above. If the
other indexes cannot be met as time being, authorities concerned shall ever increase the water quality by
arouse the people to launch patriotic health campaign and to improve the environmental sanitation, and by
effective measures for water treatment.
Reference:      Hygienic Standardfor Potable Water TJ20-76, NEPA, 1990
