Report of Environmental Engineering Member of Expert Panel (EEMEP)
on Resettlement and Environment for YRWHDC/RO
for Panel's Fifth Meeting on 3-15 October 1996
I INTRODUCTION
1.1 Work Program
On 3 October the EEMEP met with Mme. Xi Meihua for Preliminary discussions on
planning and scheduling of the work of the Environment Subpanel. On 4 October the scheduling was
set as given in Annex A, and this schedule was subsequently essentially followed. This included
provisions for the Panel's field visits on 7-9 October also shown in Annex A.
In these discussions the EEMEP recommended that the field visits for the Subpanel on
Environment (SP/E) be separated -from and be longer than the 3-day period allowed for the Subpanel
on RS (SP/RS), but is was fixed that both Sps would cover 3 days but with the Sps separated for the
second and third days.
The work done by the EEMEP included (a) review of the separate reports prepared on
ERD subcontractor prograrns, for RPDI, WRPB, and a brief report on Relics, plus ERD's overall
report, including comments and suggestions (b) participation in Panel and SP/E meetings on 4 to 15
October, with detailed notes and suggestions, (c) participation on the field visits on 7-9 October with
detailed notes and suggestions, (d) on 15-17 October to supplement the work of 3-15 October on
special tasks to assist World Bank/Dr. Daniel Gunaratnam in preparation for this visit to Zhengzhou
in November 1996 on XLD/Loan I. The EEMEP's notes on the Panel/Subpanel work are given in
Anmex B and the notes for the follow-up work by THE EEMEP in Annex F.
1.2  Teclnologv Transfer
The approach used by the EEMEP is intended, inter-alia, to transfer technology in
environmental monitoring to the RO/ERD staff, in recognition that such teclnology involves many
complex technical specialties in the field of environmental engineering. This approach is likc that of
a professor in a university class in which the professor not only lectures but gives homework
assignments and examinations which are graded. The approach has been limited to lecturing (office
and field) and to preparing detailed daily notes which are translated into Chinese the next day and
given to the ERD staff and to Environmental Subpanel members. However, The EEMEP does not
know the extent to which these notes are actually read/studied by the Chinese. Also, for optimal
technology transfer it would be helpful if each ERD/RPDI/WRPB/CYPH participant would prepare
something in writing, to be translated into English, for THE EEMEP to review and comment upon.
If ERD is to achieve its goal of proper monitoring, the business of technology transfer is essential
and The EEMEP recommends that RO/ERD give consideration to the question of how the services
of The EEMEP can best be used for this purpose in future Panel meetings.
1.3  Significance of Fifth Panel Meeting
The Fifth Panel meeting and its reports on RS and Environment are especially significant
in that WB will begin discussions with the Government in November 1996 on XLD/Loan 11 For
this purpose WB/Guna will be looking to the Fifth Panel report to finish him with a summarization
of the overall findings/conclusions/recormmendations of the Panel covering the entire period of the
Panel's operations from the First to the Fifth meeting.
A/4. 1.2-1/4



1.4  Organization of Report
The present report includes sections as follows:
(1)  Introduction (work program, report organization, references, etc.)
(2)  Institutional Aspects (ERD, ERD subcontractors, Panel/Subpanel)
(3)  Construction Zone Operations
(4)  Resettlement Host Villages
(5)   Other Issues (Relics, MIS, etc.)
(6)  Sunmnary and Conclusions
(7)  Next Panel Meeting
1.5  References Utilized
These include the reports for the past 6-month period by ERD and ERP subcontractors
and other pertinent references including previous SP/E reports.
6.   SUMMARY AND CONCLUSIONS
The work of the RO/ETD is believed to be pioneering for the construction period for major
water resource development projects in China, hence this experience represents valuable guidelines for
use for other projects in China. Some of the "lessons learned" here are already being utilized by
WB/Guna in planning for the Environmental Management Office for the pending Wanjiazhai Water
Transfer Project.
A great deal of progress has been made by the RO/ERD group over the past two years
(since the first Panel meeting) in getting organized and into gear for actual opemtions. Much has been
learned and, now that the WB/XLD/Loan II mission will be visiting in November 1996, it is time now
for RO/ERM to assess its entire experience since the beginning of the RO and to prepare a plan for
achieving needed improvements including improved use of the Environmental Subpanel. The salient
improvements recommended are the following:
(a)   Chanze in Procedure for Manaaement of Construction Zone. Operations, including
(i) requiring the CCs to submit basic drawings and monthly reports, (ii) including the specifics in all
Complaints Notices, (iii) fumishing the CCs with the specific requirements to be carried out by them for
the Chinese Worker Villages in their areas.
(b)    Upgrading of RPDI Surveillance of RS Villages, including (i) delineating RS
house/facilities design criteria to use for checking to see if houses/facilities are being properly built, (ii)
requiring village to appoint a full-time "Village Environmental Management Officer", (iii) preparation
of a manual of guidelines for telling the Officer how to do his work, including monthly reporting, (iv)
training of the Officer in this work (including making and recording weekly CR tests), and (v)
monitoring visits to the village every 2 months for the first year after moving in, then every 4 months.
(c)    Compilation of ApproRriate Standards for Construction Zones and RS Villages, by ERD
et al, to obtain a manual of guidelines on appropriate standards to be utilized in ERD's prograr.,
including and "norms" all pertinent environmental issues. This could be done by for ERD by RPDI. To
be coordinated with Item (d).
(d)    Upgrading of Reference 202 Handbook on applicable environmental laws and regulations.
This is an updating of the Handbook prepared in 1994 by R-RPDI.
(e)   Other Issues
(e. 1)  Continuing development of MIS to include environmental parameters.
(e.2)  Separate reports on relics for Henan and Shanxi provinces.
(e.3)  Updating of Reference 202 Handbook and routine use of this handbook by ERD
and subcontractors.
(e.4)  Detailed advance planning for Subpanel. field trips.
A/4.1.2-1/5



(e.5) Revised reporting system for Panel visits, with ERD preparing overal-l
report and with detailed reports (to be annexes) by each of the subcontractors.
(e.6)  Iron in RS Village Water Supply: Utilize information given in Annex
G.
(e.7) Reservoir Clearing: Utilize informnation given in Annex H.
(e.8) Disease Control in Construction Camps and RS Villages: Utilize
information in Annex I.

CHAPTER 8
TRAINING
8.1    Need for Training
Implementation of WWTP will be a pioneering project for Shanxi Province (and for all of
China) in transbasin diversion of water on a massive scale, for meeting the critical needs for
more water to support continuing growth and development of parched areas of northern
China. Because of the restively high cost of such imported water, it is essential that
conventional practices on water use, waste treatment, water pollution control, and water reuse
be reoriented to achieve an integrated comprehensive program which makes
maximum/optimal use of the precious water for all essential water uses in the service areas
(Taiyuan Service Area for Phase I) including domestic, commercial, industrial, and
agricultural needs, including balanced use of all freshwater resources (local surface water,
local groundwater, and imported water), and including institutional, legal, economic, and
financing as well as engineering aspects. The proposed plan for achieving this is discussed in
ES Chapter 5.
8.2      Recommended Training
In order to achieve this goal a training program is needed to strengthen the capabilities of the
officials involved in WWTP including YRDP, Shanxi Province, and Local Agencies, in the
various new approaches and methodologies which will be utilized. The recommended
training program inlcudes two components:
(a) A specialized training program to be an integral part of the WWTP/Phase I project,
described in the EIA Main Report Chapter 8, for training of selected staff of
participating Provincial and City agencies.
(b) A training program especially for TEPB, because of the many assignments for which
TEPB will have the lead role in preparing/implementing the recommended integrated
plan, to be financed by Technical Assistance grant of $ 2 million (to be piggy-backed
onto the WB Phase I Loan).
8.2.1 Specialized Training for participating Agencies
This program, to cost an estimated $ 438,500, will include training by foreign experts of
selected Chinese personnel who will then conduct training courses at Taiyuan for stuff of
participating agencies, plus a visit by one team to Southern California to study integrated
water use technologies developed there over the past four decades and a visit by another tearn
to the Communicable Disease Center of the USA in Atlanta, Geogia. Details are given in
Figures ES.8-1 and 2.
8.2.2 TEPB Training Program
This prograrn is described in detail in Annex H of the EIA Main Report, and is summarized
here in Figure ES.8-3. This includes provision for establishing a Revolving Fund for assisting
A/4.1.3-2/3



local industries in moving ahead with their own initiative to plan/build/operate more effective
production methods which incorporate water recycling, water reuse, and waste
minimization/clean technologies.
8.3    Value of Training Program
The proposed training program should prove to be one of the most cost-effective components
of the entire WWTP. Its total cost is small, only a very small percentage of total first-phase
investment cost, but this small investment is essential if the precious imported water is to be
wisely used. It is expected that the training program will result in more appropriate planning
decisions on investments in facilities and thus result in savings of tens of millions of dollars
over the Phase I project design life period.
A/4.1.3-2/4

ANNEX 4.2.4-1
NOTES ON REVIEW OF CULTURAL RELICS PROGRAM
BY EXPERTS PANEL IN OCTOBER 1996
(Notes by Dr. K. Connor) (Ref. 201.5)
1.    Organization
To date, preservation of cultural relics has been confined mostly areas on Henan
province below elevation 180 meters. The panel was unable to get a report from Shanxi
province.
The work of is carried out under the direction of the Provincial Cultural Relics Bureau,
supported by the Archeological Institute, Ancient Buildings Studies, Luoyang Archeological
teams, the Social Science Institute, and teams of university students. The Ancient Buildings
group handles movement and preservation of surface relics, while the Archeological Institute
and university students manage underground relics. Progress and quality monitoring is
supervised by the National Cultural Relics Bureau, with assistance form the Environmental
Resources Office and the Henan Province Social Science Institute.
2.    Progress
2.1    Underaround Relics
The most important relics will be moved, but experts stated that there is neither the
time nor the money to move all the underground relics. Relics are being classified into 3
categories. At present, the budget only cover preservation of the two highest categories. Other
experts currently are evaluating the reports prepared by the project teams to make a final
selection of relics to be moved to a new site. The preservation process for other relics will
focus on classifying, studying, and videotaping relics for future study. They are selecting some
of the more important relics for detailed study.
In Henan province, excavation of underground relics has been completed.
Classification and study are in progress. A progress report has been prepared. Annex 3 contains
a recommended outline and schedule for future reports. the following sites in Henan province
have been excavated:

ANNEX 4.2.7.10-1
INTERNATIONAL ENVIRONMENTAL AGREEMENTS
A/4.2.7. 10-1/1



ANNEX 4.2.7.10-1
INTERNATIONAL ENVIRONMENTAL AGREEMENTS
The PRC has joined with other countries in the following international environmental
agreements:
(i) UN Human and Environment Declaration (also called "Stockholm Declaration"): This was
approved by the UN Human and Environment Conference of June 16, 1972, which was attended by
China. After the conference, it was decided to have a national meeting on the subject of environmental
protection. This meeting resulted in the establishment of administration responsible for such matters, and
the development of basic environmental policies of China.
(ii) International Convention on Control of Whaling Activities: This convention was signed in
Washington on December 2, 1946 and was put into effect as of November 10, 1948. It consists of II
clauses and I annex. Revisions were made in 1956 and 959 respectively. This convention was signed by
China in September 1980.
(iii) Convention on Preservation of Cultural and Natural Values: This convention, consisting of 32
clauses, was approved in Paris on November 16, 1972, and was put into force on December 21, 1975. It
was signed by China in 1985.
(iv) International Convention on Trade of Endangered and Rare Wildlife/Plant Species: This was
approved in Washington on March 3, 1973, and was enforced as of July 1, 1975. It consists of 25 clauses
and 4 annexes. It was signed by China in April 1981. It is stipulated in the Convention that any
endangered or rare wildlife and plant species shall not be exported unless an "Exportation Permit" is
issued by the responsible administration authority and is accepted by the responsible scientific institute.
(v) International Convention on Important Wetlands, Especially Habitats of Water Birds": This
convention includes 12 clauses. It was approved in Iran on February 2, 1971, and was put into effect on
December 21, 1975. It was signed by China in 1992.
(vi) UN Convention on Maritime Laws: This was jointly signed by 117 countries inclusive China in
Jamaica on December 30, 1982. Part 12 deals with "protection and preservation of maritime
environment". Because the number of signing countries is not as many as required for enforcement, this
convention has not yet been operative.
(vii) Vienna Convention on Protection of Ozonosphere: This convention (including 21 clauses and 2
annexes) was approved in Vienna, Austria, on March 22, 1985, and became operative on September 22,
1989. It was signed by China in July 1989.
(viii) Montreal Agreement on Consumption of Ozonospheric Substances: It was signed in Montreal,
Canada, on September 16, 1987, and enforced on January 1, 1989, consisting of 20 clauses and 1 annex.
Revisions were at the second meeting of signing countries on June 29, 1990. The revised version, which
was enforced on January 1, 1992, was signed by China in June 1991.
(ix) Basle Convention on Controlling Illegal Transfer and Disposal of Dangerous Wastes: This
convention, as signed in Basle, Sweden, on March 22, 1989, and became operative on May 5, 992. It
consists of 29 clauses and 6 annexes. It was signed by China in March 1990 and was approved by the
Standing Committee of the National People's Congress in September 1991.
A/4.2.7.10-1/2



(x) International Provisions for Tropical Timber: This was approved in Geneva in November 1993,
and was signed by China in July 1986.
(xi) Convention on Earliest Notice of Nuclear Accidents: It was approved in Geneva in September
1986 and was signed by China in October 1987.
(xii) Convention on Assistance for Nuclear Accidents and Radiation Accidents: This was also
approved in Geneva in September 1986 and signed by China in October 1987.
(xiii) Convention on Preventing Maritime Pollution by Dumped Wastes and Other Polluting
Materials: This was approved in December 1972 after discussions in London, Moscow and Washington.
Revisions were made in 1978 and 1980. It was signed by Cbina in December 1985.
(xiv) International Convention on Civil Liabilities for Damages by Oil Pollution: This was approved
in Brussels in November 1969. Revisions were made in 1976, 1981 and 1984 respectively. It was signed
by China in April 1980.
(xv) Sino-Japan Agreement on Protection of Migrating birds and Habitats: This agreement was
concluded in 1981.
(xvi) Beijing Declaration: As invited by the government of the People's Republic of China, ministers
from 41 developing countries discussed the challenges faced by the international communities in
identifying the rules of environmental protection and economic development/cooperation in Beijing
during June 18 to 19, 1991. Beijing Declaration, which consisted of 32 clauses, was published on June
19, 1991.
(xvii) Rio Declaration of Environment and Development: This declaration was approved at the UN
environment and development conference held in Rio de Janeiro during June 3 to 14, 1992. This
conference was attended by a Chinese mission and was addressed by Premier Li Peng. A presentation
was made to the Standing Committee of the National People's Congress by Mr. Qu Geping, director of
National Environmental Protection Agency. And ten environmental and development countermeasures
were proposed to the Central Committee of the Party and State Council by the Ministry of Foreign
Affairs and the National Environmental Protection Agency.
(xxiii) Principle Declaration of Forest Problems: This was approved at the UN Environment and
Development Conference of June 1992.
(xix) 21th Century Agenda: This was also approved at the UN Environment and Development
Conference of June 1992.
(xx) Frame Convention on Climate Changes and Convention on Biological Variability: This was
initiated during the UN Environment and Development Conference on June 11, 1992, and was formally
signed by 153 countries. It was approved by the Standing Committee of the National People's Congress
on November 7, 19992.
A/4.2.7.10-1/3

CHAPTER 5
ENVIRONMENTAL MONITORING
5.1   Introduction
51.1   Background
Environmental monitoring is an essential part of the overall environmental program of
the project including its various components. The purpose is to ensure that the EPMs specified in
the EIA will actually be carried out including EPMs applicable to (i) final design; (ii)
construction, and (iii) O&M. The EMO is responsible for management of the overall monitoring
program, including surveillance of monitoring done by various components of the project which
have their own monitoring tasks, for example, dam safety instrumentation/monitoring,
resettlement supervision/monitoring, and disease control supervision. Overall responsibility of
environmental monitoring is with the EMO with assistance from EMO's various subcontractors
and with cooperation from various other agencies. The relationships are as shown in Figure 5. 1- 1.
The XP is a very large scale and very complex project, and incorporation of
environmental monitoring into the project is a formidable job, primarily because such
incorporation is a relatively new practice in China as in virtually all developing countries. In
many respects the environmental monitoring work being done at Xiaolangdi is pioneering for
major water research development projects in China.
The overall monitoring program being conducted by EMO now includes two
components, one for Dam Construction and the other for the Resettlement Program.
5.1.2 Monitoring of the Monitoring
It is important to recognize that the basic environmental monitoring at the construction
camps is to be done by the CCs, not by the ECIs, nor by other ERD subcontractors including
YRWRDB and YRCH. The role of the ECIs and other ERO subcontractors is to "monitoring the
monitoring" by the CCs, and for this purpose each CC must submit a detailed monthly report on
its environmental monitoring work. Similarly, each RS village should appoint an Environmental
Chief, at the working level, to be the day-to-day environmental monitoring and to prepare
appropriate monthly reports.
5.2    Dam Construction Monitoring
5.2.1  Relationship to Enzineering Supervision
EMO is its own agency with its own authorities including environmental monitoring, but
EMO's environmental supervision is also a part of overall project management. For initiating the
monitoring work relating to dam construction, the work of EMO functions through the General
Supervision Office of XECC. This relationship is shown in Figure 5.2-1.
5.2.2  Monitoring Activities
Environmental monitoring for dam construction involves three aspects or "Ms", namely
"Man", "Machine", and "Method".
"Man" means the organizers, commanders, and operators involved in project
construction activities. As the control target, "man" avoids environmental pollution and
deterioration due to inappropriate human activities such as the use of construction authority.
"Man" means fully developing people's initiatives and always maintaining a sense of "protecting
the environment at the Site."
"Machine" includes construction equipment and other facilities. These can pose many
environmental hazards, especially pollution emissions and occupation heath hazards. For
ensuring good environmental quality in the construction area, the Environmental Construction
Inspections need to exercise control of construction equipment in respect of equipment sizing,
adequacy, and operation.
"Method" means the construction organization's construction schemes and methods
adopted during construction. Proper construction organization planning and implementation can
avoid or mitigate environmental pollution and deterioration due to construction activities.
5-1



Therefore one of the monitoring tasks is to help correct construction organization schemes as
needed.
5.2.3  Environmental Construction Inspectors
Dam construction environmental supervision must be carried out in collaboration with
the engineering supervision. Individual environmental supervision engineers, called
"Environmental Construction Inspectors" (ECIs) are made available at the Site. Their
responsibilities include:
(a) Monitoring of CC's Activities: To ensure the CC will comply with all requirements
for EPMs during construction (See Section 4.2.2 for details).
(b) Keeping daily and weekly records and providing regular monthly reports on the
ECI's work.
(c) Preparing drafts of notice to CCs when these are needed to improve CC performance,
to be given to EMO for appropriate action including delivery to the CC through the Engineer
Supervision/Safety Department. Taking part in interim and completion acceptance of any part,
component, or entirety of the works, with focus on checking and seeing to it that the completed.
areas are clean and in good order, that any remaining equipment/materials/rubbish have been
properly removed, and that the ground areas that have been disturbed are properly resurfaced,
and that other needed finalization activities have been duly performed.
Additional details on the work of the work of the ECIs is given in Section 4.2.2.
5.2.4 Recommended Improvements
As noted in Section 4.2.2, considerable improvements are needed in the system for
environmental monitoring of the CCs. These include (i) recognition that the role of the EC[s is
"monitoring of the monitoring", (ii) recognition by XECC that responsibility for environmental
supervision of CCs is with RO/ERO, not with the Engineer Supervisor/Safety Department, and
(iii) requirement for each CC to submit detailed monthly environmental monitoring report.
5.3    Resettlement Monitorinf
5.3.1  Environmental Review of Resettlement (RS)
Section 4.2.3 on Environmental Review of Resettlement describes the salient parameters
involved in environmental monitoring of Resettlement. EPMs are incorporated in the entire
resettlement planning and implementation process, with attention given to land resources, house
construction and household amenities, and community infrastructures including water supply,
power, waste management, roads, schools, and clinics.
5.3.2 Overall Environmental Monitoring
The overall RS environmental monitoring program is intended to include, together with
the provisions of Section 5.3. 1, the following tasks:
(a) Provision of Water Supply for Host Sites: The host village community water supply
system, if utilized, must be strengthened to provide for additional RS needs. Water quality must
meet the sanitary standard for drinking water supply (GB5749-85) as issued by the Ministry of
Health, and water supply quantity must satisfy water demands for population growth and
economic development. Water source protection includes water quality monitoring on regular
basis.
(b) Investigation of Communicable Diseases at Host Sites: To evaluate impacts of RS
on disease incidence, and to plan and arrange for implementation of needed corrective measures.
Also, health and disease control measures are made during transfer of people.
(c)  EIA is required for any resettlement-related projects including water supply
developments, floodplain improvement works, and other facilities, and appropriate
environmental protection measures should be accordingly prepared.
(d) Eco-environmental monitoring is to be carried out in host sites on a regular basis,
ensuring appropriate ecological evolution.
(e) EIA is required for any resettlement-related industries and mines to be newly built or
expanded. All wastes produced by industries and mines must be properly managed in compliance
with relevant standards.
5-2



(f) Protective measures are to be provided for spoil excavation materials resulting from
construction of all buildings and community facilities, house and roads, avoiding additional soil
erosion. Steep slope land reclamation and deforestation are forbidden.
(g) All industries/mines and new residential sites are to be selected to avoid cultural
sites, ancient tombs, and other sensitive points. Otherwise, protective measures are taken to
excavate and protect cultural relics.
(h) Resettlement agencies are to arrange environmental education for resettlers to
understand EPMs and resettlers' role and function in public consultation. Education and
distribution of laws/regulations are to be arranged, including "Law of Environment", "Law of
Water and Soil Conservation", "Law of Wildlife Protection", and "Law of Cultural Relics
Protection", so as to raise the environmental awareness of the public.
(i) Special reports are to be made by xiang and village resettlement staff, mainly
describing any environmental improvements and problems during and after resettlement (see
Table 3.18 for details), and routine reports are prepared to describe the socio-economic
development and environmental effects after resettlement. These are collected and furnished to
provincial/municipal and YRCC resettlement offices by CROs. Also, they are to be included in
the environmental progress reports submitted by the ECIs. Such reports and corresponding
written materials are prepared on a quarterly basis.
5.3.3  Recommended [mprovements
As noted in Section 4.2.3, much more attention must be given to the problem of RS
environmental monitoring. Specific recommendations are made in Section 4.2.3 on what should
be done under Loan 2 provisions so that the monitoring will achieve the goals noted above,
including appointment of an Environmental Chief (working level chief) by each RS village who
will do the basic RS village monitoring with monthly reports. The recommended monitoring
system is shown in Figure 5.2-2. The funding for the Environmental Monitoring Chief should be
furnished by RO/ERO.
5.4    Other Environmental Monitorina
5.4.1  Introduction
In addition to the environmental monitoring described in Section 5.2 and 5.3, the overall
environmental monitoring program includes attention to all other SEIs. These are discussed
below.
5.4.2  Archeological Relics: See Section 4.2.4.
5.4.3  Earthauake Stability
As noted in Section 4.2.5, the Earthquake Stability Design Team has this subject under
continuing evaluation, and the current report of this Team is included in Section 4.2.3. The EMO
should obtain updated reports every six months.
5.4.4  Public Health: See Section 4.2.6.
5.4.5 Riverine Water Oualitv
The recommended cost-effective water quality monitoring program is described in
Section 4.2.7.3, and EMO should ask its subcontractor, YRWRPB, to modify its ongoing
monitoring program to match the Section 4.2.7.3 requirements, or make other arrangements so
this will be done.
5.4.6  Reservoir Area Clearing
A special study is recommended for evaluating this issue, as discussed in Section 4.2.7.8.
5.4.7  Precious Eco-Values
According to the Law of Environment of China, the responsibility for protection of the
wetlands in the lower reaches is with the Physical Resources Protection Department of
Henan/Shandong Environmental Protection Agencies, but monitoring is the responsibility of the
local environmental monitoring stations. Monitoring costs are included in the budget of the local
government, so no additional costs will be required to be supported by the XP. However, EMO
will furnish "monitoring of the monitoring". EMO will get annual monitoring reports from the
local environmental protection agencies in Henan and Shandong. Also, as noted in Section 6.3, a
5-3



special study for evaluation of wetlands is proposed, and this will include assessment of the
wetlands monitoring program.
5.4.8 Flood Control
The current flood control evaluation is given in Section 4.2.8.1. EMO should have this
updated every six months.
5.4.9  Public Participation
Public participation is a continuing process, and Section 4.2.9.3 includes a current
updated report on this task. The EMO should arrange for further updating every six months.
5.4.10 Other SEls
These include subjects not discussed above. These are listed under Section 4.2.7 ("Other
SEIs") and under Section 4.2.8 ("Project Benefits"). These should be reviewed annually.
5.4.11 Recommended Improvements
To date ERO has not got around to giving sufficient attention to the environmental
monitoring needs described in Section 5.4. It is timely now for ERO to begin doing this job.
5.5   Summarized Environmental Monitoring Proaram
The overall Environmental Monitoring Program is summarized in Figure 5.5-1, including
estimated costs.

Environmental Manazement
6.1   Environmental Management Related to Project Stages
The job of the EMO/ERD is to carry out the EMP as specified in the EIA, to
ensure that all EPMs and environmental monitoring will actually be carried out in the
subsequent stages of project implementation. Figure 6.1-1 shows the overall
environmental management job for a project such as XP. Figure 6.1-2 shows the
application of the Figure 6. 1-1 concept for the XP Loan 1 period including evaluation of
performance. Figure 6.1-3 shows needs for continuing EMO attention for the Loan 2
period.
The following summarizes the Environrnental Management program up to 2001:
(a) For the Remaining Construction Period (1997 to 200l
(a. 1) Continuing monitoring surveillance of major SEIS:
Dam construction.
RS design/construction/operation.
Archeological relics management.
Earthquake stability evaluations.
Flood forecasting/warning/preventation system.
(a.2) Evaluation of needs for reservoir area clearing: Special study for
1997.
(a.3) Continuing monitoring/surveillance of other SEIs, namnely 4.2.6,
4.2.7, and 4.2.8.
(a.4) Training (technology transfer) program for staff of ERD/Subs (1997-
99).
(a.5) Special environmental enhancement studies (4.2.11), 1997-1999.
(a.6) Planning, by ERD for EMO functions following completion of
construction (1999-2001).
(b) For Operation of Project Components (1997 to 2001): Continuing
monitoring/surveillance of completed RS villages. Sections 6.2 to 6.5 give
details on how these objectives are expected to be met.
6.2   Environmental Management Activities
6.2.1  Organization of EMO/ERD
Over the period since establishment of RO in 1994, an EMO, called the
Environmental Resources Division (ERD), has become established as a suboffice of the
RO, with responsibility for carrying out the EPMs specified in the EIA including
environmental monitoring. The ERD capabilities comprise the ERD staff per se plus
contractual assistance from four agencies, namely (i) RPDI, (ii) YRWRB, (iii) Yellow
River Central Hospital (YRCH), and (iv) Henan/Shanxi Archeological Institutes (HSAIs).
Details on the above capabilities, including information on assignmnents and
budgets, are given in Figures 6.2-2, 3, 4, 5, 6, 7 and 8, as follows:
Figure 6.2-1: Environmental Management Organization Chart
Figure 6.2-2: Organization of ERD
Figure 6.2-3: Organization and Staffing of ERD
Figure 6.2-4: RPDI Monitoring for Dam Construction
Figure 6.2-5: RPDI Staffing, Skills, and Assignments
Figure 6.2-6: Central Yellow River Hospital Staff Assignments
Figure 6.2-7: Water Resources Protection Bureau Assignments
6-1



Figure 6.2-8: Henan/Shanxi Archeological Institutes Assignments
6.2.2 Assignments to ERD Staff and Subcontractors
Figure 6.2-1 shows the assignments carried out by ERD by its own staff and its
subcontractors (subs), as related to the discussion of SEIs in Chapter 4. Review of the
Chapter 4 evaluations shows that EMO capabilities, including capabilities of RPDI staff
who worked on EIA/92 and are now in the ERD/RPDI subcontractor group, are
progressively increasing in terms of skills needed for the EMO job, but that (i)
considerable continuing training of these staff is highly desirable to supplement the very
limited training fumished every six months by the EEMEP, and (ii) the ERD needs to
develop/use mechanics for making more effective use of other agencies having pertinent
specialty expertise, for example, on the issue of wildlife. It should be recognized that
ERDIRPDI environmental staff are mainly generalists and when they are confronted with
problems required specialized expertise, they should bring this expertise into the picture.
As shown in Figure 6.2-1, the ERD-cum-Subs performance needs improvement in
a number of ways, as follows:
(a) The performance of the RPDI team on dam construction should be much
improved now that each of the three major CCs will prepare a detailed monthly
report to provide the RPDI team.
(b) The role of the RPDI team, now limited mostly to the dam construction area,
should be expanded to give "equal attention" to the Resettlement Program. The
work on RS by YRWRB is of little value and hardly worthwhile. The RPDI role
should include arrangement for appointment of an Environmental Officer (EO) at
each RS village, funded by ERO, who will prepare monthly reports to the RPDI
team.
(c) The work of the ERD staff and of all subs should be progressively improved
much faster through provision of the extra training noted above. This will include
training of ERD staff in how to make effective use of the expertise of other
agencies as needed, including local EPBs (for example, on precious eco-resources
issues).
(d) The work of the YRWRB as now practiced for the RS program, because it is
limited to visits once every six months, is of little value. Also, it needs
improvement for the dam construction program. The YRWRB work for the RS
villages covers only water supply mineral quality, which is only one of the various
parameters of concern, and even for this visits made only every six months are
hardly worthwhile. The work of environmental management of the RS villages
should be assigned totally to RPDI. The work of YRWRPB should be limited to
(d.1) water quality monitoring of streams in ESA, and (d.2) noise/dust/air
pollution monitoring in dam construction area, to be done every three months, for
monitoring of the monitoring done by the CCs.
(e) Special studies should be undertaken in 1997, arranged by ERD, covering
the following:
(e. 1) Reservoir clearing program (as discussed in Section 4.2.7.8).
(e.2) Environmental enhancement potentials as discussed in Section 6.3.
6.2.3 ERD Reporting
ERD reporting has been geared to match the needs of the Expert Panel, with
reports produced every six months, one month ahead of Panel Meetings. These have
given only limited attention to the recommendations made by the EEMEP.
6-2



The experience to date indicates, as noted in the Panel report for Oct. 96 (Ref.
201.5), that the best procedure is for preparation of reports every six months as follows:
(a)  ERD Overall Report
Activities of ERD Staff
Brief Review of Reports by Subs
(b)  Reports by Subs
RPDI
YRWRB
YRCH
HSAIs
These reports will all be critiqued in detail by the EEMEP, hence are of value for
technology transfer purposes.
6.2.4  Estimated Costs
Estimated costs for administrating EMO/ERD are shown in Figure 6.2-9.
6.3   Follow - Up Studies
6.3.1  Recommended Studies
The EIA/92 report recommended that some additional follow-up studies be
undertaken in order to obtain optional enhancement benefits from the XLD project. None
of these has been needed for the dam construction stage operation but these should be
carried out before dam construction has been completed. The recommended studies
include those for (i) wetlands preservation, (ii) optional water balance, (iii) reservoir
fisheries, (iv) forestry rehabilitation in area around reservoir, (v) use of the reservoir area
as a regional recreational park, and (vi) enhancement of downstream aquaculture. These
are discussed below.
(a)  Wetlands Preservation
The objection here is to evaluate the situation on precious eco-wetlands in the
lower basin, to determine the extent to which these have been impaired by drying up of
the lower Yellow River due to extraction and to flood control, and if these are serious and
if the wetlands are worth it, to recommend the most feasible plan for preservation
purposes.
(b) Optimal Water Balance
The objective here is to make an in-depth evaluation of water use needs in the
lower basin for all purposes over the next two decades, for all purposes including
irrigation, urban, industrial, and other uses, to compare these with supplies available from
surface and groundwater sources, and to develop the optional plan for use of both sources,
particularly with respect to maintaining a stable water table.
(c) Reservoir Fisheries Management
The objective here is to develop a plan for management of the reservoir fisheries
to obtain optional yields for commercial and/or sports fishing, including consideration of
use of introduced species suited to the new reservoir conditions, with use of experience
gained in fisheries from existing dams/reservoirs above XLD.
(d)  Forestry Restabilitation in Reservoir Vicinity
Since the 1970s it has become increasingly recognized in the planning of
dams/reservoirs in developing countries that, because such projects do disturb and impair
existing eco-resources (both by inundation and accelerated encroachment), the project's
overall plan should include provisions for offsetting these impairments by investing in
eco-restoration, especially in reforestation and protection against erosion in the watershed
above the dam. The objective of the study proposed here is to evaluate the situation with
6-3



XLD to determine whether such a reforestation project would be feasible and if so, to
prepare the recommended improvement plan.
(e) Regional Reservoir Recreational Park
Every new rmajor reservoir offers significant opportunities for establishment of a
regional recreational park including provisions for camping, hiking, wildlife study, sport
fishing, etc. This can be especially beneficial to the "ordinary and poor" people in the
region who can scarcely afford to seek recreation elsewhere. The objective here is to
evaluate this potential for XLD to determine whether this is feasible, and if so, to prepare
the recommended plan.
(f)  Downstream Aguaculture
The objective here is to evaluate the existing downstream aquaculture situation,
determine whether it would be feasible for the XLD project to include a component for
enhancing aquaculture, and if so to prepare the recommended plan.
(g)  Additional Special Studies
6.3.2 TORs for Reconmnended Studies
The TORs for the recommended studies are given in Annex 6-1, including tasks,
skills, timeframes, and costs. All of the studies are to be done by selected Chinese experts
in the specialties concerned, and all of the recommended plans should be prepared at the
feasibility level.
6.4   Training of EMP Participants
6.4.1 Training Need
In 1996 an EIA was completed by local Chinese institutes with World Bank
assistance for the pending Wanjiazhai Water Transfer Project, a major water resources
development project which, like XLD, involves major environmental implications (Ref
305). This proposes establishment of an EMO for implementing the recommended EMP,
and establishment of an Expert Panel to meet every six months for evaluating actual
environmental performance. Based on the pioneering experience in environmental
management at XLD since establishment of XLD/ERD in 1994, the Wanjiazhai Project
Loan Agreement incorporates provision for a program for systematic training of the staff
of Wanjiazhai EMO and of the other local participating agencies including the Shanxi
EPB and the Taiyuan city EPB. This includes both tailored classroom training at Taiyuan
plus training assignments in the USA.
6.4.2  Trainine Program
It is now realized that a tailored training program is needed for XLD for the staff
of ERD and of ERD's subcontractors, and the proposed program is as follows-.
(a) Tailored classroom training (combined with field observations) of selected
staff of ERD and of ERD's subs (RPDI, YRWRPB, YRCH), in enviromnental technology
for EIA for major dam/reservoir projects, with sessions of 10 calendar days every 3
months for period of two years, with instruction by a distinguished Chinese
Environmental Technologist.
(b) Field observation training in environmental monitoring for selected ERD
and other YR agency staff, say for 10 persons, for a period of one month with a selected
appropriate agency in the USA or elsewhere. For this purpose training with one of the
State of California's Regional Water Quality Control Boards (there are nine, one for each
major river basin in California) is recommended. Arrangements for this could be made by
a selected California Environmental Technologist who has the necessary experience and
contacts with the California Boards and who would be "USA Manager" for the visiting
group. This would require 2 man-months of the Expert Expert's time. (A recommended
6-4



candidate is Mr. Robert Lewis, retired from his former position as Chief of R& D for the
California State Water Resources Control Board, the parent organization for the nine
regional boards.)
(c) Training in Management Information System technology is being developed
by YRWHDCRO for RS management, for purpose of ensuring adequate attention to
incorporation into the system of informnation on applicable environmental parameters.
This should be included in the MIS on training program for RO's RS suboffice.
6.4.3 Estimated Costs
Estimated costs for the proposal training are given in Annex 6-2.
6.5   Expert Panel Operations
The present Expert Panel and its membership are as shown in Figure 1.3.2 of
Chapter 1. The Panel totals 9 members, including 7 Chinese and two expiates. The two
expiates are Dr. H. Ludwig, an Environmental Engineer (who is the only Environmental
Engineer on the Panel) and Dr. K. Connor (who is the only Sociologist on the Panel). The
Panel is divided into two Subpanels, one on Resettlement and the other on Environment.
The Subpanel on Environment is chaired by Prof. Liu Junde (a Resettlement Expert).
With respect to ERD's operations, the periodic visits and reports prepared by the
Environmental Engineer Member of the Expert Panel (EEMEP) (Dr. H. Ludwig) have
been valuable for purposes of training of the staff of ERD and ERD's Subs. However, in
view of the great complexity of ERD's assignment, it would be very helpful if some
additional special training be furnished as discussed in Section 6.4.
With respect to Panel membership, it would be very helpful to the EEMEP if an
additional member be appointed to the Panel, who should be a distinguished Chinese
Environmental Engineer, who could serve as Dr. Ludwig's Counterpart. An alternative is
to reestablish the panel system to be two panels, one for Environmental and one for RS.
With respect to the Panel's field trips, the procedure used to date has been to
"piggyback" the environmental field trips onto the RS field trips, but this has not
permitted sufficient time for the needed field environmental work. Hence it is
recommended that in the future the environmental and RS visits be separately arranged.
6.6   Economics of EMP
6.6.1 EMP Costs
Figure 6.6-1 gives cost estimates for implementing the EMP including costs for
ERD and all ERD subcontractors.
6.6.2 Value of EMP/EMO
Environmental management is a new kind of activity in development project
planning implementation (in both ICs and DCs). Hence, even though the EIA may
included an approved EMP including the specified environmental monitoring, there is a
common tendency in DCs for the funding for this to be deleted during project
implementation because many financial reviewers do not recognize the need or because of
fiscal problems. Moreover, over the past two decades much of the environmental
monitoring done in DCs has not been effectively utilized so that financial reviewers tend
to regard such costs as an unnecessary increase in project overhead costs. It is therefore
necessary for the EMP to include its own Benefit and Cost evaluation, to show clearly
that the proposed monitoring will "make money and not lose money." Estimating the
benefits of the monitoring is not an established methodology, but estimates can be made
by projecting the likely future situation for both "with monitoring" and "without
monitoring" cases.
6-5



Section 4.2.9.1 explains the benefits gained through incorporation of EPMs into
the overall XP plan. This notes that the estimated overall benefit cost ratio with EPMs
included is approximately 1.44 compared to 1.33 without, representing an 8 percent gain
in the ratio. As shown by experience elsewhere in many DCs, without the EMO/EMP,
much if not most of those additional benefits would not actually be realized.

CHAPTER 7
SUMMARY AND CONCLUSIONS
7.1    Summary
Figure 7.1-1 has been prepared to summarize the situation for XP in terms of environmental
performance over the period 1992 (when EIA/92 was prepared) and February 1997 (date of EIA/97).
This lists all the SEls and for each gives the following evaluations (by column members, C3 to C7):
C3 Relative Workload for EMO: For some SEIs, the primary work is to be done by the EMO
including EMO subcontractors, whereas for other SEIs (for example, Earthquake Stability) the
primary work is done by other agencies from which EMO obtains the needed information every six
months.
C4 Performance Adeguacy. 1992 - Feb. 1997: This is based on the data made available by
EMO to Expert Panel and by evaluations made by Expert Panel/EEMEP.
C5 Needs for Improvements.
C5.1 The main need is for EMO and EMO subcontractors to pay strict attention to
performance on all EPMs specified in the EIA (and incorporated into the government/WB
loan agreements), and to report upon this performance in detail every six months (in April
and October), with the April report prepared to be a strict Environmental Audit for the
preceding year.
C5.2  The needed improvements will be achieved by (i) establishing an effective
Environmental Panel, (ii) restructuring the EMO into two EMOs or sub-EMOs, one for
Environment and one for RS, (iii) requiring the EMOs to follow up on all Panel
recommendations, (iv) requiring periodic diligent reporting by the EMOs and EMO
subcontractors, and (v) furnishing appropriate technical training to all EMP participants.
C6 Pro:ected Adequacy of Performance: Assuming C5, the environmental performance of
XP should be OK for each SEI and overall, with no significant net losses in environmental values but
with great gains.
C7 Assuming EPMs are implements, all risks will be adequately managed.
7.2    Conclusions
7.2.1   Overall Conclusion
As noted in Section 7.1, it is concluded that, given the improvements noted under Item C5,
all of which are to be included in the Loan 2 Agreement, if can be expected that the XP's status in
terms of environmental protection will be upgraded from its preset level of "fair performance" to
excellent performance. Much of progress in performance has been achieved since E[A/92, but it must
be remembered that XP is a large and complex project and that the concept/program for ensuring that
the project is environmentally sound is very complex and pioneering for a major water resources
development project in China. Hence, despite the progress made to date, much more needs to be
made and, based on the "lessons learned" over the period 1992-97, the entire XP/EMP is to be
restructured and improved as noted in Section 4.2.2 and as described in detail below.
7.2.2  Details on Needed lmDrovements
7.2.2.1 Responsibility for Environmental Management
The Environmental Management job, carried out by the EMO, is now assigned to RO, which
has a sub-office called the Environmental Resources Division (ERD) which is the EMO for carrying
out the EMP (Environmental Management Plan). The work of the EMO has been reviewed every six
months by the Environmental Engineer Member of the Expert Panel on Environment and
Resettlement which was established in 1994 for reviewing and guiding the work of the RO. The
reports of the EEMEP (Environmental Engineer Member of Expert Panel) are in effect
"Environmental Audits" of the RO's work on environment. These audits show clearly that the EMO
has been able to function with only partial effectiveness, primarily because the resettlement job itself
is huge and requires the RO's attention to such a degree that insufficient attention is available for
EMO. For this reason the work of the EMO subcontractors has been only partially effective for
environmental control of dam construction, less for environmental control of resettlement, and
7 -I



lacking'in other components including attention to disease control, archeological relics, and precious
eco-resources. The alternatives for correcting this problem are either (i) strengthening RO/EMO, or
(ii) establishing an EMO/Dam Project to be managed by the Dam Engineering Office, and leaving
with RO the EMO/Resettlement.
7.2.2.2 Expert Panel Restructuring
The present situation is not very satisfactory for environmental management purposes. While
the Panel is entitled, "Expert Panel on Environment and Resettlement", with seven Chinese members,
one expert Environmental Engineer (the EEMEP), and one expert Sociologist, there are no experts in
Environmental Technology among the Chinese members. Six are RS specialists and one is a Medical
Sanitation (whose field of expertise covers only a very small part of the total SEls of concern for
XP). Hence the EEMEP has no meaningful counterparts on the Panel, and the Panel has focused its
entire program (including field visits) on RS, and the Panel has not been able to give the needed
attention to Environmental Management.
The alternatives for correction are either (i) to strengthen the existing Panel by adding some
Chinese Environmental Technologists, or (ii) reestablish the Panel structure into two panels, one on
"Environmental Management" and one on "Resettlement Management". The Environmental
Management Panel would be concerned with both the EMO/Dam Project and the EMO/RS Project.
7.2.2.3 Environmental Auditine
The reports of the EEMEP have amounted to Environmental Auditing, but have not been
officially recognized as such, hence many of the findings and recommendations of the EEMEP have
gone unheeded. It is recommended that the Panel visits continue to be made every six months, and
that the Panel reports made in April each year be classified as official Environmental Audits. There
should also be understanding that Panel's recommendations on environment, made every six months,
will be acted upon by the EMOs and duly reported upon in their 6-month reports. For this purpose the
Panel's meeting should be extended to a period of three weeks each.
7.2.2.4 Environmental Management for Dam Proiect
(a) Needed ImDrovements
The program for Environmental Management for Dam Project should include all SEls except
those relating to RS, including dam construction, control of diseases affected by building and
operating the dam, relics control, and riverine water quality monitoring. With the proposed new
EMO/Dam Project set-up, it should be readily possible to carry out the recommended needed
improvements in EMO operations related to the dam. These will include improvements in (i)
performance of the Environmental Construction Inspectors (under RPDI control), (ii) control of the
CCs, including the requirement that each CC submit a detailed monthly report on the CC's
environmental management operations, (iii) the system for preparation of competent "notices" to the
CCs on particular problems and transmittal of these to the CC without interference by XECC's
Engineering Supervisor/Safety Department, (iv) performance of the other EMO subcontractors, (v)
provision of continuing periodic expert assistance to the EMO/Dam Project, and (vi) provisions for
training of EMOlDam Project staff in environmental management technology.
(b) Details on Needed ImRrovements
(b.l) With respect to Item (i) above, each ECI should be required to make daily, weekly, and
monthly reports, and for purpose of review of this work by the Expert Panel or Sub-panel on
Environment. Typical reports should be made available (in both Chinese and English) for review by
the Panel or Sub-panel every six months.
(b.2) With respect to Item (ii), the TOR for this work is as discussed in Section 4.2.2. Also,
the CC must recognize that the CC's responsibilities are not discharged simply by subcontracting
with local Chinese agencies (such as YRCH and provincial Anti-Epidemic Stations) unless these sub-
contractors carry out all of the CC's obligations (which has not been the case).
(b.3) With respect to Item (iii), it must be made plain, for the Loan 2 period, that
responsibilities for environmental management of the CCs is with the EMO, not with the XECC
Engineering Supervisor/Safety Department, and that the role of the Safety Department is simply to
transmit the EMO instructions (and not be modify them and "water them down", as has been the
7-2



case), and that the CC's monthly environmental report will include detailed discussion on receipt of
such notices and the CC's response to them.
(b.4) With respect to Item (iv), two other YRCC agencies are involved, YRCH and
YRWRPB. First, YRCH on disease control, to date has prepared semi-annual reports only to suit
their own views on reporting whereas what is needed is a report covering all aspects of the disease
control program as specified in EIA/92 and included in the Loan I Agreement. Because of this, the
YRCH performance to date has represented only partial compliance with the EIA/92 requirements,
and it seems clear that YRCH will cover all needed work only if required to produce complete reports
on performance every six months. In particular, YRCH must immediately implement monitoring as
specified in EIA/92 (i) for evaluating rat densities in the peripheral areas around the reservoir, to
protect the people living and working in the periphery from extra hazard of haemorrhagic fever, and
(ii) for monitoring disease mosquito larval in the ESA. This work must begin promptly in order to
establish a meaningful data base representing prior XP (without project) conditions. In addition,
YRCH should review its rat killing program in the construction zone so that densities will be within
the standard throughout the year.
(b.5) With respect to YRWRPB's work, the monitoring done by YRWRPB at the RS
villages, every six months, comprising only partial evaluation of water supply quality (and not even
including the salient water quality parameter of chlorine residual at taps) is so limited and so
infrequent that it is not worthwhile. The needed monitoring is for all environmental parameters
involved in RS villages, only one of which is water supply and safety. The recommendation is to
expand the role of the RPDI/ECls to cover RS villages as well as the Dam Project. (The YRWRPB's
present role does include air pollution/dust/noise monitoring in the dam construction zone and this is
valuable but too infrequent.)
(b.6) With respect to Items (v) and (vi), the 1994-97 experience of the EEMEP has showil
that additional continuing periodic technical assistance should be made available to the EMOs, by
extending the periods for Panel Meetings from two to three weeks, plus periodic training of staff of
the EMOs and of the EMO Subcontractors in environmental management technology. The
recommended training program is given in Section 6.4.
(b.7) With further reference to YRWRPB, the YRWRPB's program for Yellow River water
quality monitoring should be immediately modified to include the parameters requested in EIA/92,
which YRWRPB has not yet got around to doing even though these modifications represent only a
small amount of work.
(b.8) The present Environmental Sub-panel, at the suggestion of the EEMEP, recommended
that EMO commission a special study by pertinent experts to evaluate the need for proceeding with
the usual type of reservoir area clearing program, because of the EEMEP's judgment that the mud to
be deposited in the reservoir may make such clearing unnecessary, at least for the deeper portions of
the reservoir. This special study was not undertaken, and clearing is scheduled to start in June 1997.
The WB and XP officials may wish to give consideration to this issue during the WB's appraisal
mission. The objective is not to "disobey" government regulations on clearing, but to conduct a study
to determine whether a modification in the regulations would be in order for XP.
7.2.2.5 Environmental Management for RS
(a) As noted above, the new RO/EMO's role should be limited to environmental
management of RS, functioning with surveillance and guidance from the Expert Panel or
Panels. The functions of the RS/EMO should be to implement the work program
described in Section 4.2.3. It is believed that this can best be done by subcontract with
RPD[, with periodic assistance from the experts noted in Section 7.2.2 (b.6) above, and
with participation in the training program mentioned in Section 6.4.
(b) RO should obtain expert guidance to assist in developing the MIS program so that RS
statistics can be readily updated on a continuing basis, including data on environmental
parameters.
7.2.2.6 Summarv of Detailed Recommendations
The recommendations made above are summarized in Figure 7.2-1.
